      (maintenance only)




5/24/2026                        SE - Software change                 38
Standard change processes –
traditional
• Software change processes depend on
      • The type of software being maintained;
      • The development processes used;
      • The skills and experience of the people involved.
• Proposals for change are the driver for system evolution.
      • Should be linked with components that are affected by the change,
        thus allowing the cost and impact of the change to be estimated.
• Change identification and implementation continues throughout
  the system lifetime.


5/24/2026                          SE - Software change                     39
The software change process




5/24/2026       SE - Software change   40
Change implementation




5/24/2026      SE - Software change   41
Change implementation
• Iteration of the development process where the revisions to the
  system are designed, implemented and tested.
• A critical difference is that the first stage of change
  implementation may involve program understanding, especially
  if the original system developers are not responsible for the
  change implementation.
• During the program understanding phase, you have to
  understand how the program is structured, how it delivers
  functionality and how the proposed change might affect the
  program.
5/24/2026                   SE - Software change                42
Emergency change requests
• Urgent changes may have to be implemented without going
  through all stages of the software engineering process
      • If a serious system fault has to be repaired to allow normal operation to
        continue;
      • If changes to the system’s environment (e.g. an OS upgrade) have
        unexpected effects;
      • If there are business changes that require a very rapid response (e.g.
        the release of a competing product).




5/24/2026                           SE - Software change                        43
The emergency repair process




5/24/2026       SE - Software change   44
Agile methods and evolution
• Agile methods are based on incremental development so the
  transition from development to change is a seamless one.
      • Change is simply a continuation of the development process based on
        frequent system releases.
• Automated regression testing is particularly valuable when
  changes are made to a system.
• Changes may be expressed as additional user stories.




5/24/2026                         SE - Software change                        45
Agile/plan-based handover problems
• Where the development team have used an agile approach but
  the maintenance team is unfamiliar with agile methods and
  prefer a plan-based approach.
      • The maintenance team may expect detailed documentation to support
        evolution and this is not produced in agile processes.
• Where a plan-based approach has been used for development
  but the maintenance team prefer to use agile methods.
      • The maintenance team may have to start from scratch developing
        automated tests and the code in the system may not have been
        refactored and simplified as is expected in agile development.


5/24/2026                        SE - Software change                       46
Topics

  Introduction

  Software maintenance
  Software reingeneering
  Managing software changes
  Legacy systems




5/24/2026                     SE - Software change   47
Legacy systems
• Legacy systems are older systems that rely on languages and
  technology that are no longer used for new systems
  development.
• Legacy software may be dependent on older hardware, such as
  mainframe computers and may have associated legacy
  processes and procedures.
• Legacy systems are not just software systems but are broader
  socio-technical systems that include hardware, software,
  libraries and other supporting software and business processes.


5/24/2026                  SE - Software change                 48
The elements of a legacy system




5/24/2026       SE - Software change   49
Legacy system components
• System hardware Legacy systems may have been written for
  hardware that is no longer available.
• Support software The legacy system may rely on a range of
  support software, which may be obsolete or unsupported.
• Application software The application system that provides the
  business services is usually made up of a number of application
  programs.
• Application data These are data that are processed by the
  application system. They may be inconsistent, duplicated or
  held in different databases.
5/24/2026                  SE - Software change                 50
Legacy system components
• Business processes These are processes that are used in the
  business to achieve some business objective.
• Business processes may be designed around a legacy system
  and constrained by the functionality that it provides.
• Business policies and rules These are definitions of how the
  business should be carried out and constraints on the business.
  Use of the legacy application system may be embedded in
  these policies and rules.



5/24/2026                   SE - Software change                51
Legacy system layers




5/24/2026       SE - Software change   52
Legacy system replacement
• Legacy system replacement is risky and expensive so
  businesses continue to use these systems
• System replacement is risky for a number of reasons
      • Lack of complete system specification
      • Tight integration of system and business processes
      • Undocumented business rules embedded in the legacy system
      • New software development may be late and/or over budget




5/24/2026                       SE - Software change                53
Legacy system change
• Legacy systems are expensive to change for a number of
  reasons:
      • No consistent programming style
      • Use of obsolete programming languages with few people available with
        these language skills
      • Inadequate system documentation
      • System structure degradation
      • Program optimizations may make them hard to understand
      • Data errors, duplication and inconsistency



5/24/2026                         SE - Software change                     54
Legacy system management
• Organisations that rely on legacy systems must choose a strategy for
  evolving these systems
      • Scrap the system completely and modify business processes so that it is no longer
        required;
      • Continue maintaining the system;
      • Transform the system by re-engineering to improve its maintainability;
      • Replace the system with a new system.
• The strategy chosen should depend on the system quality and its business
  value.




5/24/2026                               SE - Software change                                55
Figure 9.13 An example of a legacy
system assessment




5/24/2026       SE - Software change   56
Legacy system categories
• Low quality, low business value
      • These systems should be scrapped.
• Low-quality, high-business value
      • These make an important business contribution but are expensive to maintain.
        Should be re-engineered or replaced if a suitable system is available.
• High-quality, low-business value
      • Replace with COTS, scrap completely or maintain.
• High-quality, high business value
      • Continue in operation using normal system maintenance.




5/24/2026                               SE - Software change                           57
Business value assessment
• Assessment should take different viewpoints into account
      • System end-users;
      • Business customers;
      • Line managers;
      • IT managers;
      • Senior managers.
• Interview different stakeholders and collate results.




5/24/2026                     SE - Software change           58
Issues in business value assessment
    • The use of the system
            • If systems are only used occasionally or by a small number
              of people, they may have a low business value.
    • The business processes that are supported
            • A system may have a low business value if it forces the use
              of inefficient business processes.
    • System dependability
            • If a system is not dependable and the problems directly
              affect business customers, the system has a low business
              value.
    • The system outputs
            • If the business depends on system outputs, then the
              system has a high business value.
5/24/2026                                SE - Software change               59
System quality assessment
1. Business process assessment
      • How well does the business process support the current goals of the
        business?
2. Environment assessment
      • How effective is the system’s environment and how expensive is it to
        maintain?
3. Application assessment
      • What is the quality of the application software system?




5/24/2026                          SE - Software change                        60
1. Business process assessment
• Use a viewpoint-oriented approach and seek answers from system
  stakeholders
      • Is there a defined process model and is it followed?
      • Do different parts of the organisation use different processes for the same function?
      • How has the process been adapted?
      • What are the relationships with other business processes and are these necessary?
      • Is the process effectively supported by the legacy application software?
• Example - a travel ordering system may have a low business value
  because of the widespread use of web-based ordering.




5/24/2026                                SE - Software change                                   61
2. Factors used in environment assessment
            Factor               Questions
            Supplier stability   Is the supplier still in existence? Is the supplier financially stable and
                                 likely to continue in existence? If the supplier is no longer in business,
                                 does someone else maintain the systems?
            Failure rate         Does the hardware have a high rate of reported failures? Does the
                                 support software crash and force system restarts?
            Age                  How old is the hardware and software? The older the hardware and
                                 support software, the more obsolete it will be. It may still function
                                 correctly but there could be significant economic and business
                                 benefits to moving to a more modern system.
            Performance          Is the performance of the system adequate? Do performance
                                 problems have a significant effect on system users?




5/24/2026                                           SE - Software change                                      62
2. Factors used in environment assessment
            Factor                 Questions
            Support requirements   What local support is required by the hardware and
                                   software? If there are high costs associated with this
                                   support, it may be worth considering system replacement.
            Maintenance costs      What are the costs of hardware maintenance and support
                                   software licences? Older hardware may have higher
                                   maintenance costs than modern systems. Support software
                                   may have high annual licensing costs.
            Interoperability       Are there problems interfacing the system to other systems?
                                   Can compilers, for example, be used with current versions
                                   of the operating system? Is hardware emulation required?




5/24/2026                                 SE - Software change                                   63
3. Factors used in application assessment

            Factor              Questions
            Understandability   How difficult is it to understand the source code of the current
                                system? How complex are the control structures that are used?
                                Do variables have meaningful names that reflect their function?
            Documentation       What system documentation is available? Is the documentation
                                complete, consistent, and current?
            Data                Is there an explicit data model for the system? To what extent is
                                data duplicated across files? Is the data used by the system up to
                                date and consistent?
            Performance         Is the performance of the application adequate? Do performance
                                problems have a significant effect on system users?




5/24/2026                                    SE - Software change                                    64
3. Factors used in application assessment
            Factor                 Questions
            Programming language   Are modern compilers available for the programming
                                   language used to develop the system? Is the programming
                                   language still used for new system development?
            Configuration          Are all versions of all parts of the system managed by a
            management             configuration management system? Is there an explicit
                                   description of the versions of components that are used in
                                   the current system?
            Test data              Does test data for the system exist? Is there a record of
                                   regression tests carried out when new features have been
                                   added to the system?
            Personnel skills       Are there people available who have the skills to maintain the
                                   application? Are there people available who have experience
                                   with the system?



5/24/2026                                  SE - Software change                                     65
System measurement
• You may collect quantitative data to make an assessment of the
  quality of the application system
      • The number of system change requests; The higher this accumulated
        value, the lower the quality of the system.
      • The number of different user interfaces used by the system; The more
        interfaces, the more likely it is that there will be inconsistencies and
        redundancies in these interfaces.
      • The volume of data used by the system. As the volume of data (number
        of files, size of database, etc.) processed by the system increases, so
        too do the inconsistencies and errors in that data.
      • Cleaning up old data is a very expensive and time-consuming process

5/24/2026                          SE - Software change                       66
Key points
 • Software development and evolution can be thought of as an integrated,
   iterative process that can be represented using a spiral model.
 • For custom systems, the costs of software maintenance usually exceed
   the software development costs.
 • The process of software evolution is driven by requests for changes and
   includes change impact analysis, release planning and change
   implementation.
 • Legacy systems are older software systems, developed using obsolete
   software and hardware technologies, that remain useful for a business.

Build tools - The problem
• The transition from source code to the executable application contains many
  mechanical steps:

  • Settings required paths and libraries
  • Compiling source code
  • Copying source files (e.g. images, sound files, start scripts)
  • Setting of file permissions (e.g. to executable)
  • Packaging of the application (e.g. zip, tar, dmg, docker image)
• Executing  these steps manually is time-consuming and the chance of
  introducing failures is high - Any manual activity is a possible source of error

                           Compiler                                        Linker

Make
• Available on almost any platform

• Executes rules and tasks defined in a
  Makefile

• Manages dependencies between tasks
  ( tree structure)

• Check the timestamps to execute only
  what is actually needed

Apache Ant
• Based on Java, so cross-platform

• Tasks are java class

• Can be customised writing new plugin (
  adding new java classes)

• A lot of built-in and custom task
  available

• Build files are XML document

    • Lot of tools to edit

    • Easy to understand and to maintain

• Build files are platform independent

Apache Maven




               23
Maven Overview
• A Java project management and integration build tool,
  http://maven.apache.org/
• Maven supports the same functionality as Ant
      • Ant is actually integrated into Maven
• Maven’s extended functionality
      • Automatically generates a development website containing
             • Project organization information
             • Project development state reports
• Based on the concept of a central project object model (POM)

05/04/2026                                  Development tools      24
Maven Capabilities
• Maven is able to
      • Receive developer artifacts from the programmers’ Source Code
        Mamangement directory
      • Compile and package the source code
      • Download dependent artifacts from another (external) repository
      • Generate Javadoc websites
      • Generate developer and code metrics
      • Generate websites containing status information to track issues
      • Deploy the compiled source code and the generated website to a
        webserver


05/04/2026                         Development tools                      25

    • Project structure
    • Strict lifecycle

Modeling a Project in Maven
         Project Object Model (POM)
             • Project name, id, description, version number
             • Website URL
             • Issue tracking URL
             • Mailing list URLs
             • SCM directory URL
             • Developer information
             • License information
             • Dependencies
                • Name, version, location of a Java library from jar file
             • Project directory layout
                • Specifies source code and unit test code directories

Project Descriptor (in project.xml)
               <project>
               <!-- a unique name for this project -->
                 <id>arena</id>
                 <!-- a short but descriptive name for the project -->
                 <name>ARENA</name>
                 <currentVersion>0.9</currentVersion>
                 <!-- details about organization who 'owns' the project -->
                 <organization>
                   <name>Bernd Bruegge &amp; Allen H. Dutoit</name>
                   <url>http://wwwbruegge.in.tum.de/</url>
                 </organization>
                 <inceptionYear>2004</inceptionYear>
                 <logo>/images/arena.gif</logo>
                 <description>ARENA</description>
                 <!-- the project home page -->
                 <url>http://sysiphus.informatik.tu-muenchen.de/arena</url>
                 <siteAddress>sysiphus.informatik.tu-muenchen.de
                 </siteAddress>
                 <siteDirectory>/www/arena</siteDirectory>

     By defaut, on maven-central.
     However, the <repository> tag is declared inside the <repositories>
     element, which lists all the additional repositories you want Maven to use
     to resolve dependencies.


05/04/2026                                    Development tools                   43
Apache Maven dependencies


• Dependency management:
  • declarative
  • transitive
  • scope aware
     1. Compile (default): The dependency is needed for compilation and execution.
     2. Provided: The dependency is provided by the container or runtime environment, so it's
        not necessary to include it in your project.
     3. Runtime: The dependency is only required at runtime, but not during compilation.
     4. Test: The dependency is only used for testing purposes.




44
Apache Maven dependencies
                              <dependencies>
                                  <dependency>
                                     <groupId>group-a</groupId>

Example                              <artifactId>a-artifact-id</artifactId>
                                     <version>1.0</version>
                                     <scope>compile</scope>
                                     <dependencies>
                                         <!-- B is a runtime dependency of A -->
A (compile) depends on B (runtime)       <dependency>
                                            <groupId>group-b</groupId>
                                            <artifactId>b-artifact-id</artifactId>
                                            <version>2.0</version>
                                            <scope>runtime</scope>
                                         </dependency>
                                     </dependencies>
                                  </dependency>
                              </dependencies>

Build Management in the project.xml
              The Build tag specifies the programmer’s
               directory containing the source code to be used
               for the build
              <build>
                  <sourceDirectory>src</sourceDirectory>
                  <resources>
                       <resource>
                           <directory>src</directory>
                           <includes>
                               <include>**/*.properties</include>
                               <include>**/*.gif</include>
                           </includes>
                       </resource>
                  </resources>
              </build>

05/04/2026   Development tools   49
Do not commit IDE files
• IDE files are local and personal
• They add noise and merge conflicts
• They reduce portability
• Source control should track code, not tools




5
0
Maven Must Stay IDE‑Independent

• Maven is the build abstraction
• It must run from CLI and CI
• IDEs read Maven, not the opposite
• A project must build with: mvn clean install
    without opening any IDE
• No IDE dependency → reproducible builds

5
1
Formatting as Part of the Build (Spotless)

• Code formatting differences generate noise in Git diffs
• IDE-specific formatting leads to inconsistent style across the team
• A Maven formatting plugin makes formatting: automatic |
  repeatable | IDE-independent
• Spotless Maven Plugin:
     enforces a single formatting standard
     runs locally and in CI
     stores formatting rules as code in pom.xml

• Result: Git tracks intent, not whitespace

5
2
Formatting as Part of the Build (Spotless)
             <plugin>
               <groupId>com.diffplug.spotless</groupId>
               <artifactId>spotless-maven-plugin</artifactId>
               <version>2.43.0</version>
               <executions>
                 <execution>
                   <goals>
                     <goal>check</goal>
                     <goal>apply</goal>
                   </goals>
                 </execution>
               </executions>
               <configuration>
                 <java>
                   <googleJavaFormat/>
                 </java>
               </configuration>
             </plugin>

The problem
• Multiple people are working on the same source code
• More than one version of the software has to be supported
• When has been introduced that bug?
• Who made the change?

We need version control to:
• To keep track of changes
• To keep track of versions
• To know who broke the code
• To revert a change
• To allow more people to work on the same software concurrently

Subversion


             • Open Source Project http://subversion.tigris.org/
                • Commands: checkout, add, delete, commit, diff
                • Version controlled moving, renaming, copying of
                  files and directories
                • Version controlled metadata of files and directories
             • Server Options
                • Standalone installation
                • Integrated into the Apache webserver

SVN architecture




05/04/2026         Development tools   68
SVN Global Revision Number Scheme




             The global revision number applies to
             the entire tree
             The revision number is increased for
             each check in, regardless of the
             extent of the changes.

       Continuous Integration (CI)
       Continuous integration (CI) is the practice of automating the integration of
       code changes from multiple contributors into a single software project.
       It’s a primary DevOps best practice, allowing developers to frequently merge
       code changes into a central repository where builds and tests then run.
       Automated tools are used to assert the new code’s correctness before
       integration.
       Especially valuable in agile approach


                                                                                            Iterative &
                              Waterfall                                                     incremental

Plan                                                             Analysis                   Analysis                      Analysis
          Analysis
                     Design                                      Design                     Design                        Design




                                                                            Deploy




                                                                                                          Deploy




                                                                                                                                       Deploy
                                  Code                           Code                       Code                          Code
                                          Test




                                                          Plan




                                                                                     Plan




                                                                                                                   Plan
                                                 Deploy          Test                       Test                          Test




                                                                                                                                     101
Continuous Integration Motivation
• Risk #1: The later integration occurs in a project, the bigger is
  the risk that unexpected faults occur
• Risk #2: The higher the complexity of the software system, the
  more difficult it is to integrate its components
Continuous integration addresses these risks by building as early
as possible and frequently
• Additional Advantages:
  • There is always an executable version of the system
 • Team members have a good overview of the project status
                                                                      102
CI can regularly answers these questions
• Do all the software components work together?
• How much code is covered by automated tests?
• Where all tests successful after the latest change?
• What is my code complexity?
• Is the team adhering to coding standards?
• Where there any problems with the last deployment?

    12/05/2026                        14 - design to code                                  5
    Increase Inheritance
• Rearrange and adjust classes and operations to
  prepare for inheritance
  • Generalization: Finding the base class first, then the sub
    classes
  • Specialization: Finding the the sub classes first, then the
    base class
• Generalization is a common modeling activity. It
  allows to abstract common behavior out of a group of
  classes                                                         Introduce inheritance
  • If a set of operations or attributes are repeated in 2        when it supports:
    classes the classes might be special instances of a more      • shared behavior/state
    general class                                                 • polymorphism (client
• Always check if it is possible to change a subsystem               code uses base type)
  (collection of classes) into a superclass in an                 • future variation (new
                                                                     subclasses later)
  inheritance hierarchy.
   12/05/2026                         14 - design to code                        6
     Generalization: Finding the super class
• You need to prepare or modify your classes for generalization
• All operations must have the same signature but often the signatures do not
  match initially
• Superclasses are desirable. They
   • increase modularity, extensibility and reusability
   • improve configuration management
• Many design patterns use superclasses
   • Try to retrofit an existing model to allow the use of a design pattern.

When you introduce a superclass, methods need consistent names/parameters, otherwise
you can’t use polymorphism cleanly.
Example: if one class has pay(float amount) and another has makePayment(Money m), you
can’t directly put them under a common pay(...) abstraction until you normalize the signatures.
A big part of “design to code” is aligning method signatures so polymorphism works.


    12/05/2026                           14 - design to code                           7
Transformation: Increase Inheritance /Generalization
Generalization is not a GoF pattern itself, but it enables several GoF
patterns that rely on polymorphism and abstraction.
1) Template Method (Behavioral). You factor an algorithm skeleton into
   a superclass and let subclasses fill in steps  generalizing common
   behavior and ensuring consistent signatures.
2) Strategy (Behavioral). You generalize an interface and allow multiple
   interchangeable implementations (algorithms)  Strategy depends
   on a clean generalization (interface) and consistent signatures.
3) Factory Method (Creational). Generalization sets up a product
   hierarchy so factories can return the base type  you need a
   polymorphic base type to make Factory Method work well.

   12/05/2026                   14 - design to code                  8
MODEL TRANSFOMATION: Collapsing Objects
  Object design model before transformation:


                        Person                                  SocialSecurity
                                                               number:String
         Object design model after transformation:

                                                 Person
                                        SSN:String


                 Turning an object into an attribute of another object is
                 usually done, if the object does not have any interesting
                 dynamic behavior (only get and set operations).

  12/05/2026                             14 - design to code                     9
To Collapse or not to Collapse?
• Collapse a class into an attribute if the only operations defined on
  the attributes are Set() and Get().
If a class:
• has no meaningful behavior,
• doesn’t participate in interactions
• is just a data wrapper…
then it may be better represented as a simple attribute/value.
Collapse “anemic objects” (data-only) into attributes to reduce
complexity
A class should generally represent something with: behavior, rules, invariants, or
lifecycle. If it doesn’t, it adds overhead (extra associations, extra objects) without
value. But sometimes “value objects” do have behavior (e.g., validating SSN
format), so we might keep them as classes anyway

12/05/2026                             14 - design to code                               10
To Collapse or not to Collapse?
This transformation is not a GoF pattern by itself. It’s a model
simplification refactoring that:

• reduces the number of classes/objects,
and
• removes “data-only” classes that don’t earn their complexity.

However, it supports GoF usage indirectly by keeping the design
model clean and focused on objects that actually have behavior.


12/05/2026                    14 - design to code                  11

 Delaying expensive computations

GoF pattern: Proxy (Structural).

 12/05/2026               14 - design to code                 13
 Model refinement

Moving from “design” to “code” isn’t a straight line: we often
need model-level refactoring first, i.e., model
transformations to improve:
• inheritance structure (generalization/specialization)
• simplicity (collapse trivial classes)
• performance-friendly design (proxy/lazy loading)
…and then you implement. This is exactly what “Design to
Code” means: refine the model until it’s ready for
implementation.

   Refactoring: Pull Up Field
If multiple subclasses define the same field, move it up into their common superclass.
Introduce User.email and removes the duplicate email fields from Player, LeagueOwner, and Advertiser.
                                                       public class User {
  public class Player {                                  private String email;
      private String email;                            }
      //...                                            public class Player extends User {
  }                                                        //...
  public class LeagueOwner {
                                                       }
      private String eMail;
                                                       public class LeagueOwner extends
      //...                                              User {
  }                                                        //...
  public class Advertiser {                            }
      private String                                   public class Advertiser extends
      email_address;                                     User {
      //...                                                //...
  }                                                    }
   12/05/2026                                 14 - design to code                                       16
Refactoring: Pull Up Field
 Before: three classes each define their own “email” field, but with different names
 After: a superclass User is introduced with a single private String email; field, and
   the three classes now extend User
 This addresses a very common case: duplicated state across sibling classes.
  When you see the same concept stored in multiple sibling classes (here: email),
  it causes:
 • Duplication: same data exists in multiple places (harder to maintain)
 • Inconsistency risk: different naming (email, eMail, email_address) suggests
   different rules might accidentally emerge (validation, formatting, null handling).
 • Poor polymorphism: you can’t treat all these as “has email” in a uniform way
   without repeating logic elsewhere.
 Pull Up Field resolves that by making “email” part of the shared abstraction
 (User).

12/05/2026                            14 - design to code                                17
Refactoring: Pull Up Field, step-by-step
 • Identify the shared concept: email exists in all three subclasses (even if named differently).
 • Normalize the name: choose a single canonical attribute name (email).
 • Introduce a superclass (User) containing the field.
 • Make subclasses extend the superclass and remove their local email fields.
 • (Optional best practice) Provide access through methods (getters/setters) rather than direct
   field access.
 Which improvements?
 Single source of truth. All users now store email in one place (User.email). If validation rules
   change, you implement them once.
 Better extensibility. If you add a new user-like class later (e.g., Referee, Coach), it can inherit
   email automatically.
 Enables patterns and polymorphism. Once you have a common User, other structures become
   simpler:
       • collections like List<User>
       • shared logic such as sendEmail(User u)
       • and many GoF patterns that benefit from base types (e.g., Template Method, Factory Method) become
         easier to apply.
 Be aware about Visibility and Semantic mismatch.

12/05/2026                                      14 - design to code                                          18
    Refactoring: Pull Up Constructor
If subclasses repeat the same initialization, move that shared initialization into a superclass constructor
and call it with super(...). User(String email) centralizes initialization; subclasses delegate.

   public class User {                               public class User {
     private String email;                             public User(String email) {
   }                                                         this.email = email;
                                                       }
   public class Player extends User {                }
       public Player(String email) {
             this.email = email;               public class Player extends User {
       }                                                     public Player(String email) {
                                                                    super(email);
   }                                                                <<other things>>
   public class LeagueOwner extends                          }
       User{                                   }
       public LeagueOwner(String email) {      public class LeagueOwner extends User {
                                                             public LeagueOwner(String email) {
             this.email = email;                                    super(email);
       }                                                     }
                                               }
   }                                           public class Advertiser extends User {
   public class Advertiser extendsUser{                      public Advertiser(String email) {
       public Advertiser(String email) {                            super(email);
                                                             }
             this.email = email;               }
       }
 12/05/2026                              14 - design to code                                              19
   }
Refactoring: Pull Up Constructor
 After you “pull up” a field, you frequently see the next duplication:
 • Every subclass constructor repeats the same initialization logic
   (this.email = email)
 This duplication is bad because:
 • If you later change how email is stored/validated (trim whitespace,
   lowercasing, null checks), you must update every constructor.
 • It violates DRY (Don’t Repeat Yourself).
 So, you pull up the initialization to the superclass constructor.
 • Constructors of subclasses should call super(...). If the base class owns
   the base state (like email), the base class constructor should initialize it,
   and subclasses should just forward the value.
 • Subclasses keep “their own” initialization. Player can still do “other
   things” after calling super(email). That’s the right structure:
       • base initialization first
       • subclass-specific initialization next
12/05/2026                                  14 - design to code                    20
Benefits of Pull Up Constructor
 • Centralized invariants and validation
 If you decide email must be normalized:
 this.email = email.trim().toLowerCase();
 you do it once (in User). Every subclass inherits that rule automatically.
 • Consistent object creation
 Every User subtype is guaranteed to have email initialized the same way.
 • Cleaner subclasses
 Subclasses stay focused on what makes them different instead of
 repeating boilerplate.
 1. “We see repeated attributes → restructure the design model to
     introduce a superclass.”
 2. “We see repeated initialization → restructure constructors so the
     superclass owns its state.”
12/05/2026                        14 - design to code                         21
Topics

  Model transformations

  Code refactoring

  Forward engineering (from model to class)

                  Create class definitions

                  Realize inheritance

                  Map dependencies

                     Map associations

  Model-driven engineering


12/05/2026                                    14 - design to code   22
     Mapping Designs to Code
• Objective. Implementing the object design model in a programming language:
   • Class and interface definitions
   • Method definitions
• Work from OOA/D artifacts
   • Create class definitions from Design Class Diagrams (DCDs)
       • A Domain Model is analysis-level (“real world concepts”).
       • A Design Class Diagram is design-level and contains:
             •    software classes (including controllers, services, adapters)
             •    method signatures
             •    design patterns
             •    navigability/dependencies
   • Mapping inheritance: generalization (inheritance): arrow from subclass to superclass
   • Mapping dependencies: a dependency is a “uses” relationship
   • Mapping associations: an association represents a structural link between objects: “has-
     a” relationship, often long-lived reference, may have multiplicity (1, 0..1, 0.., 1..)

 12/05/2026                                              14 - design to code                                         26
From Interaction diagram to method
implementation
A) Sequence diagram messages become code statements. A message in a sequence diagram is a
method call (often with a return value). Mapping rules:
- Message with return: `x := obj.m(a,b)` → `Type x = obj.m(a,b);`
- Message without return: `obj.m(a,b)` → `obj.m(a,b);`
So:
- `desc := getProductDescription(id)` becomes a variable assignment
- `makeLineItem(desc, qty)` becomes a direct call
B) The code snippet’s naming is illustrative, not perfect Java naming. The slide shows
`catalog.ProductDescription(id)` but conceptually it means “ask catalog for a product description for
id.”
In Java we’d implement:
ProductDescription desc = catalog.getProductDescription(id);
currentSale.makeLineItem(desc, qty);
C) This provides a mechanical way to go from design to code. Sequence diagrams are not just
documentation; they’re a blueprint for method bodies.
If you have a detailed interaction diagram, you can almost “transcribe” it into Java: each message
turns into a code line in the same order.

       Collection classes
    Out-of-the-box
    collection
    classes from
    java.utils

- List (e.g., ArrayList) → ordered, duplicates
allowed
- Set (e.g., HashSet) → uniqueness, no
duplicates
- Map (e.g., HashMap) → key → value lookup
(e.g., `ItemID → ProductDescription`)
- Others: Queue/Deque, TreeMap/TreeSet for
ordering, etc.
Don’t invent custom container classes when
standard ones exist. Use standard library
collections for multiplicity relationships.

  12/05/2026                                             14 - design to code         ...                                  30
    Class definitions


implement from least-coupled to most-coupled
  A class is “less coupled” if it depends on fewer other classes.
  - `Payment` and `ProductDescription` are usually simple and
  depend on basic types respectively Money and (Text, Money,
  ItemID)
  - `SalesLineItem` depends on `ProductDescription`
  - `Sale` depends on `SalesLineItem` and `Payment`
  - `Register` depends on `Sale` and `ProductCatalog`
  - `Store` depends on `Register` and may aggregate sales
  So, the best order is to implement the “leaf” dependencies first
  (low coupling), then the higher-level orchestration classes last.

  12/05/2026                   14 - design to code                    31
    Class definitions


implement from least-coupled to most-coupled
  This helps in practice because:
  - You reduce compile errors (dependencies exist when you need them)
  - You can unit test early building blocks in isolation
  - You avoid implementing orchestration code before you have stable domain
  code

  This mirrors real engineering workflow: build foundations first, then integrate.

  When implementing from a DCD, follow dependency direction: implement
  low-level, low-coupled classes first; implement high-coupled “coordinator”
  classes last.


  12/05/2026                         14 - design to code                        32
Summary and EXAMPLE
1. Class diagram → class skeletons: fields come from attributes +
associations
2. Interaction diagram → method bodies: each message becomes a
statement (assignment/call/creation)
3. Multiplicity → collections: use `List/Set/Map` to represent `1..*` etc.
4. Use standard Java collections (`java.util`)
5. Implement bottom-up by coupling: least-coupled classes first, most-
coupled last.

12/05/2026                                    14 - design to code   34
Forward Engineering: Mapping a UML
Model into Source Code
         • Goal: We have a UML-Model with inheritance.
           We want to translate it into source code
         • Question: Which mechanisms in the
           programming language can be used?
             • Let’s focus on Java
         • Java provides the following mechanisms:
             • Overwriting of methods (default in Java)
             • Final classes
             • Final methods
             • Abstract methods
             • Abstract classes
             • Interfaces.
12/05/2026                           14 - design to code   35
Forward Engineering: Mapping a UML
Model into Source Code
             “Forward engineering” = systematically mapping UML
             concepts into language constructs.
             Inheritance is one of the most important UML concepts, and
             Java offers multiple ways to express it, depending on what
             you want to allow:
             • Do you want subclasses to be able to override behavior? If
               yes → normal methods (overridable by default).
             • Do you want to forbid overriding? Use final methods or final
               classes.
             • Do you want to force subclasses to implement some
               operations? Use abstract methods and an abstract class (or
               use an interface).
             So, we can conclude: UML “inheritance” is not just one Java
             feature; it maps to a set of features depending on whether you
             want specialization, reuse, contract, or restrictions.
12/05/2026                             14 - design to code                    36
    Forward Engineering Example
          Object design model before transformation:

                          User                                             LeagueOwner
                  -email:String                                       -maxNumLeagues:int
                  +getEmail():String                                  +getMaxNumLeagues():int
                  +setEmail(e:String)                                 +setNaxNumLeagues(n:int)
                  +notify(msg:String)

          Source code after transformation:

Forward Engineering Example
1) UML class → Java class
`User` and `LeagueOwner` map to Java classes.
2) UML visibility `-` (private) → Java `private`
UML `-email:String` becomes `private String email;`
3) UML operations `+` (public) → Java `public`
`+getEmail()` becomes `public String getEmail()` etc.
4) UML inheritance/generalization → Java `extends`
`LeagueOwner` inherits from `User` using Java’s
`extends`.
5) Why getters/setters
UML frequently lists attributes + operations; in Java,
direct access to private fields is not possible from
outside, so getters/setters implement that access path.
This example demonstrates the simplest, most direct
mapping: UML class diagram → Java class skeleton with
`extends`, fields, and accessors.

                                                  14 - design to code                                  42
  Example for the use of Abstract Methods
• Problem: Delivery a general encryption method
• Requirements:
    • The system provides algorithms for existing encryption methods (e.g.
      Caesar, Transposition)
    • New encryption algorithms, when they become available, can be linked into
      the program at runtime, without any need to recompile the program
    • The choice of the best encryption method can also be done at runtime.
Moving from “mechanisms” (extends, final, interface, abstract) to “design
intent”. There are two overlapping design needs:
• Shared workflow (encrypt/decrypt a text of words), same across
  algorithms
• Variable step (encode/decode a single word), different per algorithm
This is classic Template Method thinking: put the fixed algorithm in the superclass, make
variable steps abstract and require subclasses to implement them.
  12/05/2026                           14 - design to code                           43

      • encrypt() encrypts a text of words

      • decrypt() deciphers a text of
        words
      • encode() uses a special algorithm           +encode()              +encode()
        for encryption of a single word             +decode()              +decode()
      • decode() uses a special algorithm
        for decryption of a single word.

Dependency IN DOMAIN MODEL
             • A dependency relationship denotes a semantic relationship between
               model elements, where a change in the supplier class may cause a
               change in the client class.
             • Semantics focuses on the relation between signifiers, such as words,
               phrases, signs, symbols, ….
             • What would happen if java.sql changed, where all of our, say,
               persistency mechanisms use (depend on) the objects in this package?
             • Need to
                • Determine what causes the supplier to be visible to the client


                              Client                                     Supplier



12/05/2026                                         14 - design to code                48
Dependency IN DOMAIN MODEL
1) Dependency = “If you change Supplier, Client might break”
In UML, A depends on B means A uses B in a way such that:
• changes to B (its interface/signature/contract) may force changes to A.
This is weaker than an association. It typically means temporary usage
rather than “has-a” structure.
2) “Semantic relationship” → not about runtime pointers, about meaning and
compilation coupling
Semantics is mentioned because dependencies are about:
• types, method signatures, imports, contracts
• not necessarily stored references.
If you use java.sql.Connection in method signatures, you have a
dependency: if its API changes, your code changes.
3) “Visibility” is the key diagnostic:
To know whether A depends on B, ask: How is B visible to A?
12/05/2026                      14 - design to code                      49
   CLASS COMMUNICATION
Four “communications pathways” to supplier; that is, how are suppliers made ‘visible’ to clients.
“Visibility” means: the client class contains a reference to the supplier type in one of the following places

    • Local variable reference – inside a method
        • Supplier object is declared locally (created temporarily during execution of an operation / method)
        • Client object declares a local variable within a specific method within client.
    • Parameter reference – inside a method signature (parameter/return)
        • supplier object is a parameter to, or the return class of an operation (within some method) in the
          client object.
        • A method in client has a formal parameter of type supplier or the method returns an object of type
          supplier…
               • (think: File Reader…. These return other objects dealing with I/O…)
    • Global reference – supplier object is global (static/singleton).
        • Self-explanatory
    • Field reference – The supplier object is a data member in the client object.
        • There is an instance variable within object of type supplier.

  12/05/2026                                           14 - design to code                                  50
 Local Variable Visibility  Dependency
     • The op1() operation contains a local variable (an object) of type
       ClassB (local variable is not a parameter)
     • Hence there is a dependency between these two classes: ClassA
       depends on ClassB.

 Local Variable Visibility  Dependency
     This is a dependency (not association)
     • The reference to ClassB exists only temporarily during method
       execution.
     • ClassA instances do not own/keep a ClassB reference as part of
       their state.
     • In UML, this is modeled as a dependency dashed arrow A B.
     In Java terms
     This is common when:
     • you instantiate a helper (new ClassB()) inside a method, or
     • you store a returned object in a local variable.
     This creates compile-time coupling to ClassB (import/type reference),
     hence dependency.

                   class Register                                           public void enterItem(itemID, qty)

This visibility is required because in the enterItem diagram a Register needs to send the
getProductDescription message to a ProductCatalog, therefore Register must have a field reference.
“design-to-code” rule: if object A repeatedly sends messages to object B across system operations, A
must be able to reference B, often as a field (association).
So you can derive associations from interaction diagrams: if Register must call ProductCatalog as
part of its responsibility, then Register must “know about” the catalog, which means storing it (or
having some other visibility mechanism).
    12/05/2026                                                          14 - design to code                           59
     MAP DEPENDENCIES
Dependency (weaker, often temporary)
Occurs when supplier is visible via:
• local variable inside a method
• parameter/return type in method signatures
• global access (singleton/static)
Modeled in UML as dashed arrow Client Supplier.

Association (stronger, structural)
Occurs when supplier is visible via:
• field / instance variable (part of object state)
Modeled as solid association line (with multiplicities).

When you see two classes in design/code and you must decide dependency vs association,
ask:
• Does the client store the supplier as a field?→ Association.
• Is the supplier only used as a local variable or parameter/return type?→ Dependency.
• Is the supplier accessed globally (static/singleton)?→ Dependency (with global visibility).

Mapping UML associations into Java code

 1. What fields/collections you need to add to
    classes
 2. Where to put them (one side vs both sides)
 3. How to keep bidirectional links consistent (the
    hard part)
 4. Which Java collections are typically used.


 12/05/2026             14 - design to code           62
 Mapping Associations
1. Unidirectional one-to-one association
2. Bidirectional one-to-one association
3. Bidirectional one-to-many association
4. Bidirectional many-to-many association
5. Bidirectional qualified association.

In UML, an “association” is a relationship between classes/objects.
When you implement it in code you must decide:
• Does only one class need to “know” the other? (unidirectional)
• Or must both know each other? (bidirectional)
• How many objects are on each side? (multiplicity: 1, 0..1, *, etc.)
Each combination leads to a different field/collection pattern in code.

    Bidirectional one-to-many association
      Object design model before transformation:
                                     1                    *
                 Advertiser                                           Account

Source code after transformation:                 public class Account {
                                                      private Advertiser owner;
public class Advertiser {
  private Set accounts;                               public void setOwner(Advertiser newOwner) {
  public Advertiser() {                                    if (owner != newOwner) {
         accounts = new HashSet();                               Advertiser oldOwner = owner;
  }                                                              owner = newOwner;
  public void addAccount(Account a) {
         accounts.add(a);                                        if (newOwner != null)
         a.setOwner(this);                                             newOwner.addAccount(this);
  }                                                              if (oldOwner != null)
  public void removeAccount(Account a) {
         accounts.remove(a);                                           old.removeAccount(this);
         a.setOwner(null);                                 }
     }                                                }
}                                                 }




    12/05/2026                             14 - design to code                                      66
Bidirectional one-to-many association
- One `Advertiser` has many `Account`s (`1` to `*`)
- Each `Account` has one owner (`Advertiser`)
So, it’s bidirectional: you can navigate both:
- Advertiser → accounts
- Account → owner

Standard approach about maintaining bidirectional consistency
1) Multiplicity “many” → use a collection
Because Advertiser has many accounts, you need a collection:
- private Set accounts = new HashSet();
12/05/2026                   14 - design to code                67
Bidirectional one-to-many association
`Set` choice:
- prevents duplicates automatically
- often appropriate when you care about “membership” not ordering (if
  ordering matters, you might use `List`.)

2) The “two-way update” problem
If you do only: `advertiser.accounts.add(account)`
and forget: `account.owner = advertiser`,
then the association becomes inconsistent.
So you need rules like:
- Whenever you add an account to an advertiser, set the account’s owner
- Whenever you set an account’s owner, ensure the advertiser contains the
account
This is why both `addAccount` and `setOwner` call each other.
12/05/2026                      14 - design to code                         68
Bidirectional one-to-many association
3) Avoid infinite recursion / repeated updates
Notice the guard:
if (owner != newOwner) { ... }
Without it, you’d get loops like:
- addAccount → setOwner → addAccount → setOwner → …
The guard ensures the update happens only when there’s a real change.

4) Handling reassignment properly
The code stores the old owner and updates both sides:
- Remove from old owner
- Add to new owner
That is exactly what shall be done when ownership changes in a 1–many
relationship.
Bidirectional 1–many mapping requires a collection on the “many” side and careful
synchronization logic so both ends stay consistent on add/remove/transfer.
12/05/2026                         14 - design to code                          69
Non è possibile visualizzare l'immagine.




                                           Bidirectional many-to-many association
                                            Object design model before transformation
                                                                             * {ordered}         *
                                                           Tournament                                         Player

                                            Source code after transformation
                                             public class Tournament {                public class Player {
                                                 private List players;                   private List tournaments;
                                                 public Tournament() {                   public Player() {
                                                      players = new ArrayList();                  tournaments = new ArrayList();
                                                 }                                       }
                                                 public void addPlayer(Player p) {       public void addTournament(Tournament
                                                                                         t) {
                                                      if (!players.contains(p)) {                 if (!tournaments.contains(t)) {
                                                             players.add(p);                        tournaments.add(t);
                                                             p.addTournament(this);                 t.addPlayer(this);
                                                      }                                           }
                                                 }                                       }
                                             }
                                           12/05/2026                                 }
                                                                                      14 - design to code                           70
 Bidirectional many-to-many association
1) Many-to-many means collections on both sides:
- A `Tournament` has many `Player`s - Tournament.players
- A `Player` participates in many `Tournament`s - Player.tournaments
Often with `{ordered}` meaning the association is ordered.
2) Why `List` and why checks?
The slide’s UML includes `{ordered}`, that’s why a `List` is used
instead of a `Set`.
However, `List` allows duplicates, so a check is used
if (!players.contains(p)) { ... }
to prevent accidental duplication.
 12/05/2026                   14 - design to code                71
 Bidirectional many-to-many association
3) Same “two-way update” principle
Just like 1–many, the key challenge is consistency:
- `Tournament.addPlayer(p)` must ensure `Player` also references
the tournament
- `Player.addTournament(t)` must ensure `Tournament` references
the player
So, each method calls the other — again requiring the `contains`
guard to avoid infinite loops.

Bidirectional many-to-many is implemented as two collections +
mutual update methods + duplication guards.

 12/05/2026                  14 - design to code                 72
 Map associations
1) Navigability decides where fields live
   • Unidirectional: field on one side only
   • Bidirectional: fields/collections on both sides
2) Multiplicity decides field vs collection
   • 1 / 0..1 → single reference
   • * / 1..* → collection (List/Set)
3) Bidirectional associations require consistency management
The code patterns always do:
   • update “this side”
   • update “other side”
   • protect against loops (if owner != newOwner, if !contains)
4) Prefer making associations immutable where possible (simplifies
everything)
The bidirectional 1–1 slide explicitly avoids setters by establishing the
association in constructors and “never modifying” it.
 12/05/2026                             14 - design to code                 73

  Model transformations

  Code refactoring

  Forward engineering (from model to class)

                  Create class definitions

                  Realize inheritance

                  Map dependencies

                     Map associations

  Model-driven engineering


12/05/2026                                    14 - design to code   74
Model-based Software Engineering
• The Vision
     • During object design we build an object design model that realizes the
       use case model and which is the basis for implementation (model-
       driven design)
• The Reality
     • Working on the object design model involves many activities that are
       error prone
     • Examples:
             • A new parameter must be added to an operation. Because of time pressure it is added to
               the source code, but not to the object model
             • Additional attributes are added to an entity object, but the data base table is not updated
               (as a result, the new attributes are not persistent).

12/05/2026                                       14 - design to code                                     75
Model-based Software Engineering
“model-code mismatch”
• In an ideal world, the model is the “single source of truth.”
• In the real world, people change code quickly under time pressure and the model
  becomes outdated.
Why it happens
• Models are often updated manually (extra work).
• Teams prioritize working code over documentation.
• Different artifacts evolve at different speeds (code vs UML vs DB schema).
If your process relies on a model being accurate, you need mechanisms to keep it
synchronized:
      code generation (forward engineering)
      reverse engineering to refresh models
      automated checks (CI pipelines, schema migration tools)
      disciplined change management (update model as part of “definition of done”)

12/05/2026                                  14 - design to code                       76
OO development is iterative

       • OOA/D artifacts feed into implementation model in
         a traceable manner
       • Some tools generate partial code from UML
       • But programming is not trivial generation!
       • Programmers make changes as the work out the
         details
       • Therefore, Expect and plan for change and
         deviation from design during programming

12/05/2026                     14 - design to code           77
OO development is iterative
       1) Your design is a hypothesis
       Design models are not perfect. When coding starts, you discover:
             •   missing attributes
             •   missing edge cases
             •   performance concerns
             •   integration constraints
       2) Traceability is helpful but not absolute
       Traceability means you can connect:
             • a method → a sequence diagram message
             • a class → a DCD class
             • a DB column → an entity attribute
       But it doesn’t mean the model never changes.
       3) Tools can generate “skeletons,” not complete software
       Code generation may create:
             • class headers
             • method stubs
             • fields…but cannot always generate business logic, validation rules, error handling,
               transaction behavior, etc.
       • Treat design and code as co-evolving artifacts.
       • Plan for “feedback loops” from implementation back into design.
12/05/2026                                           14 - design to code                             78
Model-driven engineering
• Model-driven engineering (MDE) is an approach to software development where
  models rather than programs are the principal outputs of the development
  process.
• The programs that execute on a hardware/software platform are then generated
  automatically from the models.
• Proponents of MDE argue that this raises the level of abstraction in software
  engineering so that engineers no longer have to be concerned with programming
  language details or the specifics of execution platforms.
• (LOW CODE / NO CODE)
  https://www.economist.com/business/2022/01/29/what-if-all-workers-wrote-
  software-not-just-the-geek-elite

12/05/2026                         14 - design to code                           79
Model-driven engineering
Think of MDE as “models are the product” and code is a generated artifact.
Key characteristics
• The model is detailed enough to generate executable code.
• A toolchain transforms models into platform-specific implementations.
Why it’s appealing?
• You can focus on what the system should do rather than syntax and platform plumbing.
• If generators exist for different platforms, you could “port” by regenerating.
Why low-code/no-code mention?
Low-code platforms are essentially a form of MDE:
• you describe behavior visually/declaratively
• the platform generates code or executes a model internally
MDE is a “raise the abstraction level” philosophy: you model more, code less (or code is
generated).
12/05/2026                                14 - design to code                              80
Usage of model-driven engineering
• Model-driven engineering is still at an early stage of
  development, and it is unclear whether or not it will have a
  significant effect on software engineering practice.
• Pros
     • Allows systems to be considered at higher levels of abstraction
     • Generating code automatically means that it is cheaper to adapt
       systems to new platforms.
• Cons
     • Models for abstraction are not necessarily right for implementation.
     • Savings from generating code may be outweighed by the costs of
       developing translators for new platforms.

12/05/2026                         14 - design to code                        81
Usage of model-driven engineering
Pros
1. Higher abstraction reduces cognitive load
     • Instead of dealing with runtime details everywhere, you reason in a smaller set of
       concepts.
2. Portability by regenerationIf you have:
     • Model → Java generator
     • Model → C# generator, then moving platforms becomes easier (in principle).
Cons (the real-world friction)
1. The “abstraction gap”: a model that is great for communication can be
   too vague for correct code generation.
2. Generator/tooling costBuilding and maintaining “translators” (generators)
   is expensive:
     • new platform versions
     • new frameworks
     • changing runtime
MDE trades manual coding effort for toolchain engineering effort and
requires a good fit between model abstractions and implementation needs.
12/05/2026                               14 - design to code                                82
Types of model
             • A computation independent model (CIM)
                • These model the important domain abstractions used in a
                  system. CIMs are sometimes called domain models.
             • A platform independent model (PIM)
                • These model the operation of the system without reference
                  to its implementation. The PIM is usually described using
                  UML models that show the static system structure and how
                  it responds to external and internal events.
             • Platform specific models (PSM)
                • These are transformations of the platform-independent
                  model with a separate PSM for each application platform.
                  In principle, there may be layers of PSM, with each layer
                  adding some platform-specific detail.

12/05/2026                               14 - design to code                  83
Types of model
   Why these layers exist?
   Essence of MDA (Model-Driven Architecture), a structured form of MDE:
   • CIM (what the business wants)
         • Focus: domain meaning, not computation details
         • Example in flight booking: “Booking,” “Passenger,” “Ticket,” “Flight,” “Seat” with
           business semantics
   • PIM (what the system must do, still technology-agnostic)
         • Adds software behaviors: system events, responsibilities, interactions
         • Still avoids committing to “Java + Spring” or “.NET + EF.”
   • PSM (how it’s implemented on a specific platform)
         • Now you commit to platform specifics:
             •   Java/Spring annotations
             •   SQL schema mapping
             •   REST controllers
             •   message broker specifics
   CIM → PIM → PSM is a conceptual pipeline to manage complexity and
   portability: separate domain, design, and platform concerns.
12/05/2026                                  14 - design to code                                 84
MDA transformations


                                                                          Visual depiction of
                                                                          transforming between
                                                                          these model layers:
                                                                          CIM → PIM → PSM
                                                                          and then to code
                                                                          (typical MDA toolchain).



             A transformation is a set of rules that “refines” a model:
             • add platform details
             • map abstract types to platform types
             • generate artifacts like code/config/DB schema
             The key is: transformation is systematic, not ad-hoc.
12/05/2026                              14 - design to code                             85
Multiple platform-specific models
                                                                 You can have multiple
                                                                 PSMs derived from the
                                                                 same PIM, one per
                                                                 platform (e.g., web,
                                                                 mobile, embedded,
                                                                 different tech stacks)




     Why multiple PSMs is important?
     This is the “portability promise” of MDA:
     • Keep one platform-independent design (PIM)
     • Derive multiple platform-specific realizations (PSMs)
     • Generate multiple codebases / deployables from a shared
        model core
     In practice it’s hard, but that’s the principle

     •GitHub Copilot: AI-powered coding assistance

12/05/2026                                         14 - design to code                                          87
Model-driven engineering
1) Why model-driven is attractive?
     •       abstraction, traceability, portability (in principle)
2) Why it’s hard in practice?
     •       model-code drift, generator cost, abstraction mismatch
3) How MDA structures the approach
     •       CIM → PIM → PSM transforms progressively toward implementation
4) How AI changes the game
     •       AI provides a new kind of “forward engineering support,” but doesn’t
             remove the need for engineering discipline

12/05/2026                                   14 - design to code                    88
Key points
  • We described transformations on the model and on the code, between
    model and code
  • The most relevant transformation is model-to-code, or forward engineering
  • Some tools allow for a (partial) automation of this process.

  Programming isn’t “trivial generation.” Tools help, but humans still:
  • resolve edge cases
  • integrate libraries
  • ensure performance and correctness
  • handle deployment constraints

        5/3/2026                      CODE    12 - GoF patterns                                      3
Relation between GRASP and GoF
• GRASP → responsibility assignment

• GoF → reusable design solutions

Typical flow:
1. Use cases
2. Domain model
3. GRASP responsibility assignment
4. GoF patterns refine the design
5. Code


5/3/2026                     12 - GoF patterns   4
Three Types of GoF Patterns
• Creational patterns:
      • Deal with initializing and configuring objects
• Structural patterns:
      • Composition of classes or objects
      • Decouple interface and implementation of classes
• Behavioral patterns:
      • Deal with dynamic interactions among societies of objects
      • How they distribute responsibility




5/3/2026                                12 - GoF patterns           5
Creational Patterns
           • Singleton: Guarantee access to a singular (sole) instance
           • Simple Factory: Create specialized, complex objects
           • Abstract Factory: Create a family of specialized factories
           • Factory Method: Define an interface for creating an object,
             but let subclasses decide which class to instantiate
           • Builder: Construct a complex object step by step
           • Prototype: Clone new instances from a prototype
           • Lazy initialization: Delay costly creation until it is needed



5/3/2026                             12 - GoF patterns                       6
Structural Patterns
           • Adapter:
               • Converts interface of a class into one that clients expect
           • Bridge:
               • Links abstraction with many possible implementations
           • Composite:
               • Represents part-whole hierarchies as tree structures
           • Decorator:
               • Attach additional responsibilities to object dynamically
           • Facade:
               • Simplifies the interface for a subsystem
           • Flyweight:
               • Shares many fine-grained objects efficiently
           • Proxy:
               • Provides a surrogate or placeholder for another object to
                 control access to it

5/3/2026                                 12 - GoF patterns                    7
Behavioral Patterns
           • Chain of Responsibility:
               • Request delegated to the responsible service provider
           • Command:
               • Request or Action is first-class object, hence storable
           • Iterator:
               • Aggregate and access elements sequentially
           • Interpreter:
               • Language interpreter for a small grammar
           • Mediator:
               • Coordinates interactions between its associates
           • Memento:
               • Snapshot captures and restores object states privately
5/3/2026                             12 - GoF patterns                     8
Behavioral Patterns (cont.)
• Observer:
   • Observers update automatically when observed object changes
• State:
   • Object whose behavior depends on its state
• Strategy:
   • Abstraction for selecting one of many algorithms
• Template Method:
   • Algorithm with some steps supplied by derived class
• Visitor:
   • Operations applied to elements of a heterogeneous object structure


5/3/2026                        12 - GoF patterns                         9
Abstract class vs. interface




5/3/2026           12 - GoF patterns   10
Strategy-style: interface is better
Use case: multiple interchangeable algorithms (GoF Strategy)

public interface PaymentMethod {
  boolean pay(double amount);
}

public class CreditCardPayment implements PaymentMethod {
  public boolean pay(double amount) { /* ... */ return true; }
}

public class PayPalPayment implements PaymentMethod {
  public boolean pay(double amount) { /* ... */ return true; }
}

5/3/2026                              12 - GoF patterns          11
Template Method: abstract class is better
Use case: shared workflow with variation points (GoF Template Method)

public abstract class AbstractIdDocument {
  protected String documentNumber;

    public final boolean validate() {
      return hasValidFormat() && notExpired();
    }

    protected abstract boolean hasValidFormat();

    protected boolean notExpired() { /* shared check */ return true; }
}

public class Passport extends AbstractIdDocument {
  protected boolean hasValidFormat() { /* passport rules */ return true; }
}
