Adapter
Problem: How to resolve incompatible interfaces or provide a
 stable interface to similar components with different interfaces?
Solution: Convert original interface component into another one
 through an intermediate adapter.




5/3/2026                     12 - GoF patterns                   14
Adapter
• The adapter translates calls to its interface into calls to the original
  interface, and the amount of code necessary to do this is typically small.

There are two types of adapter patterns:
• Object Adapter pattern and
• Class Adapter pattern (by implementing interfaces and implementations
  inheritance).




5/3/2026                          12 - GoF patterns                            15
Object Adapter Pattern
 • In this type of adapter pattern, the adapter contains an instance of the class it
   wraps (Adaptee) so it can call the method, methodB(). In this situation, the
   adapter makes calls to the instance of the Adaptor via adaptor.methodA(), which
   invokes adaptee.methodB().




                                                                      Still, not the best solution…



 • The adapter hides the adaptee's interface from the client.

Object Adapter Pattern               See Adapter1
                                     Code example

                                                    <<interface>>                  volume()

                                                Sphere                   Torus                 TimsTorus

We want to create a hierarchy of three-dimensional shapes, and we’d like to use an existing
implementation of calcVolume in TimsTorus. (TimsTorus is already in its own hierarchy)                See Adapter3
We cannot modify the code in TimsTorus because it is being used by others, and thus cannot            code xample
make it a subclass of ThreeDShape.
So, we develop Torus (an <<Adapter>>) such that its instances have a link to the instance of
TimsTorus and delegate all operations to TimsTorus.

Adapters are sometimes called Wrappers. Java wrapper classes Integer, Float, Double, etc
  are adapters for the Java primitive types.
      5/3/2026                                                12 - GoF patterns                                19
                                                                                                 See Adapter2
    Class Adapter                                                                                Code example

• This type of adapter uses multiple polymorphic interfaces : The adapter implements or inherits both the
  interface that is expected and the interface that is pre-existing.
• It is typical for the expected interface to be created as a pure interface class, especially
  in languages such as Java that do not support multiple inheritance.




    5/3/2026                                        12 - GoF patterns                                       20
This is NOT Facade
Facade intent (GoF definition)
• Provide a unified interface to a set of interfaces in a subsystem.
Facade simplifies complexity, it does not adapt it.

Key characteristics of Façade
• Wraps multiple objects
• Offers high-level operations
• Does not change interfaces
• Client usually still could access subsystem objects directly

• Facade does NOT translate interfaces
• Facade represents a subsystem — Adapter represents one adaptee
• Facade defines new services, Adapter preserves old ones

Topics
  Introduction

  adapter

  singleton

  factory

  strategy

  composite

  facade

  observer

  command



5/3/2026         12 - GoF patterns   22
SINGLETON
      • Context:
           • It is very common to find classes for which only one instance should exist
             (singleton)
                • Examples: a Main Window; Company or University class.
      • Problem:
           • How do you ensure that it is never possible to create more than one instance of a
             singleton class?
      • Forces:
           • The use of a public constructor cannot guarantee that no more than one instance
             will be created.
           • The singleton instance must also be accessible to all classes that require it


5/3/2026                                   12 - GoF patterns                                 23
                                    Ensure a class has
                                    only one instance                   «Singleton»

Singleton                           and provide a global
                                    point of access to it.

5/3/2026                                            12 - GoF patterns                                                       24
Singleton
• There is criticism of the use of the singleton pattern, as some
  consider it an anti-pattern, judging that it is overused, introduces
  unnecessary restrictions in situations where a sole instance of a
  class is not actually required, and introduces global state into an
  application

• The Abstract Factory, Builder, and Prototype patterns can use
  Singletons in their implementation.

• Façade objects are often Singletons because only one Façade
  object is required.

5/3/2026                         12 - GoF patterns                       25
       Singleton                                                                                       See Singleton
                                                                                                       Code example



class MySingleton {
      // Static variable reference of single_instance of type MySingleton
      private static MySingleton single_instance = null;

       // Declaring a variable – String in this example
       public String s;

       // Constructor - Here we will be creating private constructor restricted to this class itself
       private MySingleton()
       {
          s = "Hello I am a string part of MySingleton class";
       }

       // Static method to create instance of MySingleton class
       public static MySingleton getInstance()
       {
          if (single_instance == null)
              single_instance = new MySingleton();

           return single_instance;
       }
   }
       5/3/2026                                                          12 - GoF patterns                             26
Simplest fix: synchronized getInstance()

Thread-safe but slower
PRO: Thread-safe
CONS: Synchronization cost on every call

    // Thread-safe using synchronization
    public static synchronized Singleton getInstance() {
       if (single_instance == null) {
           single_instance = new Singleton();
       }
       return single_instance;
    }

5/3/2026                           12 - GoF patterns       27
Simplest fix: synchronized getInstance()
It works
• synchronized ensures only one thread can execute
  getInstance() at a time
• Prevents multiple instances from being created

Drawback
• Every call is synchronized, even after the instance exists
• Can be inefficient in performance-critical systems

Correct but not optimal

5/3/2026                     12 - GoF patterns                 28
Better solution: Double-Checked Locking
Thread-safe
Efficient (synchronization happens only once)

class Singleton {
   private static volatile Singleton single_instance = null;
…
   public static Singleton getInstance() {
      if (single_instance == null) {         // First check (no lock)
          synchronized (Singleton.class) {
             if (single_instance == null) {  // Second check (with lock)
                 single_instance = new Singleton();
             }
          }
      }
      return single_instance;
   }

5/3/2026                                       12 - GoF patterns           29
Better solution: Double-Checked Locking
Thread-safe
Efficient (synchronization happens only once)

class Singleton {
   private static volatile Singleton single_instance = null;
…
   public static Singleton getInstance() {
      if (single_instance == null) {         // First check (no lock)
          synchronized (Singleton.class) {
             if (single_instance == null) {  // Second check (with lock)
                 single_instance = new Singleton();
             }
          }
                                                         • First check avoids locking after initialization
      }                                                  • Second check guarantees only one instance is created
      return single_instance;                            • volatile prevents instruction reordering issues in
   }                                                        multithreaded Java

5/3/2026                                     12 - GoF patterns                                       30
Best solution: Initialization-on-Demand Holder
Thread-safe
Lazy
No synchronization overhead
Clean design

class Singleton {
…
private static class SingletonHolder {
     private static final Singleton INSTANCE = new Singleton();
   }

   public static Singleton getInstance() {
     return SingletonHolder.INSTANCE;
   }

5/3/2026                                     12 - GoF patterns    31
Best solution: Initialization-on-Demand Holder

Lazy
No synchronization overhead
Clean design

class Singleton {
…
private static class SingletonHolder {
     private static final Singleton INSTANCE = new Singleton();
   }

   public static Singleton getInstance() {                       • Uses class loading guarantees
     return SingletonHolder.INSTANCE;                            • JVM loads SingletonHolder only when
   }                                                               getInstance() is called
                                                                 • Class loading is thread-safe by definition

5/3/2026                                      12 - GoF patterns   41
Factory Method
Definisce un’interfaccia per la creazione di un oggetto, lasciando
alle sottoclassi la decisione sulla classe che deve essere
istanziata.
Il pattern Factory Method consente di deferire l’istanziazione di
una classe alle sottoclassi.
• una classe non può sapere in anticipo le classi degli oggetti da creare
• una classe vuole che le sue sottoclassi scelgano gli oggetti da creare
• le classi delegano la responsabilità ad una o più classi helper e si colloca
  la conoscenza di quali classi helper vengano delegate in un punto ben
  preciso

5/3/2026                           12 - GoF patterns                             42
Factory Method
• Useful when a class needs an object from a hierarchy but doesn’t know which
  subclass it should create until runtime/configuration
• Useful when the client (or “creator”) knows it needs a Product but doesn’t know
  the exact ConcreteProduct to instantiate (depends on config/environment/state)
• Useful when you want to avoid spreading new ConcreteProduct() in many places
  and keep creation in a dedicated variation point (factory method)
Creator: declares the factory method, and contains the “business method” that
  uses the created product.
ConcreteCreator(s): override the factory method to create a specific
  ConcreteProduct.
Product: interface/abstract class defining what the created objects can do.
ConcreteProduct(s): implementations of the product interface.


5/3/2026                            12 - GoF patterns                           43
Factory Method

At times, an application object may only know that it needs to
access a class from within the class hierarchy, but does not know
exactly which class from among the set of subclasses of the
parent class is to be selected.
The choice of an appropriate class may depend on factors such
as:
• The state of the running application.
• Application configuration settings.
• Expansion of requirements or enhancements.

5/3/2026                    12 - GoF patterns                   44
Factory Method
• Context/Problem
       Who should be responsible for creating objects when there might be
        the choice between several different object to be created?


• Solution
       Create a method to handle the creation. (a.k.a. Virtual Constructor)
       Define an interface for creating an object, but let subclasses decide
        which class to instantiate. Factory Method lets a class defer
        instantiation to subclasses


5/3/2026                             12 - GoF patterns                          45
Factory Method
Factory Method pattern recommends encapsulating the
functionality required to select and instantiate an appropriate
class, inside a designated method referred to as a factory
method.
A factory method can be defined as a method in a class that:
– Selects an appropriate class from a class hierarchy based on
the application context and other influencing factors.
 – Instantiates the selected class and returns it as an instance of
the parent class type.

5/3/2026                      12 - GoF patterns                       46
Factory Method
Benefits
• Add a new product type by adding a new
  ConcreteCreator/ConcreteProduct, without rewriting the core
  workflow.
• Better low coupling: creator depends on the Product
  abstraction, not concrete classes.
Typical drawbacks
• More classes (Creator + ConcreteCreators + Products) →
  slightly higher complexity.
• Can be overkill if we only need one-off object creation with no
  variation.

      Abstract factory
      • Abstract Factory is a creational design pattern that lets you
        produce families of related objects without specifying their
        concrete classes.


• Factory Method usually creates
  **one product type** (e.g., `Button`).
• Abstract Factory creates **multiple
  related product types** (e.g.,
  `Button`, `Checkbox`, `TextField`)
  that must match the same “family”
  (Windows/Web/Mac).

      5/3/2026                             12 - GoF patterns            59
Abstract factory
• Produce families of related objects without specifying their
  concrete classes. Think: not one product, but a coherent set of
  products that must match.
• Useful when your system must create multiple related product
  types (e.g., Button + Checkbox + Menu) that must be consistent
  with a “family/variant” (e.g., Windows UI family vs Web UI
  family).
• Useful when you want to select a “family” once
  (Windows/Web/Mac) and then create everything in that style
  without repeating conditionals.


5/3/2026                     12 - GoF patterns                  60
Abstract factory
AbstractFactory: declares creation methods for each
 product in the family (e.g., createButton(),
 createCheckbox(), …).
ConcreteFactoryX: implements those methods to create
 the concrete family (e.g., WindowsFactory returns
 WindowsButton, WindowsCheckbox…).
AbstractProduct(s): interfaces for each product type
 (Button, Checkbox, …).
ConcreteProducts: the actual implementations per family.

5/3/2026                  12 - GoF patterns                 61
Abstract factory
Benefits
• Guarantees compatibility/consistency within a family (we
  won’t mix WindowsButton with WebCheckbox by mistake).
• Centralizes complex creation logic and can hide
  initialization/caching strategies (factories as cohesive
  helpers).
Typical drawbacks
• Adding a new family (e.g., “Mac”) is easy, adding a new
  concrete factory + products but adding a new product type
  (e.g., createSlider()) can be harder because you must
  modify all factories to support the new product.


5/3/2026                   12 - GoF patterns                  62
    Factory class – example 3

                                  12 - GoF patterns
Factory class – example 3
Instead of having **one factory** with `if/else`, we can pick a
**concrete factory** once:
• choose `WindowsButtonFactory` → always produces Windows-
  style components
• choose `WebButtonFactory` → always produces Web-style
  components
This avoids repeated branching and makes it easy to add new
families:
- add `MacButtonFactory`
- add `MacButton`
…and existing code doesn’t change.

Abstract factory

5/3/2026          12 - GoF patterns   66
FACTORY - ADVANTAGES
• Separates responsibility of complex creation into cohesive
  helper classes
• Hides complex creation logic, such as initialization from a file
• Handles memory management strategies,
  such or recycling or caching

5/3/2026                                                                    12 - GoF patterns                                               69
Factory Method and Abstract Factory
Very often used together, because they solve different layers of
the same “creation variability” problem
• Factory Method
      • Controls which concrete product to create.
      • Variation is handled by subclassing the creator (polymorphism).
      • Typical form: a base class defines a workflow and calls createX(),
        subclasses override createX().
           • Think: “Which Button implementation should I create?”
• Abstract Factory
      • Controls which family of related products to create (a “suite”).
      • Variation is handled by choosing a factory object (composition).
      • Typical form: a factory interface has multiple creation methods:
        createButton(), createCheckbox(), etc.
           • Think: “Windows family vs Web family vs Mac family.”
5/3/2026                                12 - GoF patterns                    70
Factory Method and Abstract Factory
Why combine them?
When we have both:
• Families of products (Windows/Web/Mac), and
• Multiple products per family (Button, Checkbox, Menu, …), and
• A need to keep client code stable
…then Abstract Factory gives you the family selection, and
Factory Method is a great way to implement each “creation
method” cleanly and extensibly.

Abstract Factory chooses the family; Factory Method
chooses/creates each concrete product inside that family.

5/3/2026                    12 - GoF patterns                     71
Factory Method and Abstract Factory
Abstract Factory defines multiple factory methods
interface GUIFactory {
   Button createButton();
   Checkbox createCheckbox();
}
each method is a “factory method” (a method that creates an object)


Concrete factories implement those factory methods
class WindowsFactory implements GUIFactory {
   public Button createButton() { return new WindowsButton(); }
   public Checkbox createCheckbox() { return new WindowsCheckbox(); }
}…
5/3/2026                         12 - GoF patterns                      72
Adapter and Singleton
The Adapter pattern lets classes with incompatible interfaces
work together by converting the interface of an existing class (the
adaptee) into one the client expects (the target).

Singleton Pattern ensures a class has exactly one instance and
provide a global access point to it.
• Private constructor → prevents new from outside the class.
• Private static field → holds the single instance.
• Public static getInstance() → returns the single instance (often
  created lazily).

5/3/2026                      12 - GoF patterns                      73

  Introduction

  adapter

  singleton

  factory

  strategy

  composite

  facade

  observer

  command



5/3/2026         12 - GoF patterns   74
Strategy Pattern
     The Strategy pattern defines a family of algorithms,
     encapsulates each one in its own class, and makes
     them interchangeable.
     It lets the algorithm vary independently from the clients
     that use it.

     In simple words:
     Same goal, different ways to do it — and we want to
     choose/change the way at runtime without if/else
     everywhere.

5/3/2026                        12 - GoF patterns                75
Strategy Pattern
     Use Strategy when we see one (or more) of these
     symptoms:
     • We have a method full of conditionals selecting
       behavior
     • We need to switch behavior at runtime (e.g., “fast” vs
       “memory-saving”).
     • We often add new variants of the same behavior
       (pricing rules, sorting algorithms, routing policies,
       payment methods, validation rules).


5/3/2026                        12 - GoF patterns               76
Strategy Pattern
     • Different algorithms exists for a specific task
           • We can switch between the algorithms at run time
     • Examples of tasks:
           • Different collision strategies for objects in video games
           • Parsing a set of tokens into an abstract syntax tree (Bottom
             up, top down)
           • Sorting a list of customers (bubble sort, merge sort, quick
             sort)
     • Different algorithms will be appropriate at different
       times
           • First build, testing the system, delivering the final product
     • If we need a new algorithm, we can add it without
       disturbing the application or the other algorithms.
5/3/2026                                12 - GoF patterns                    77
    Example : Decide Algorithms at Runtime
                          Client                                Idea: client wants to decide at
                                                                runtime which kind of sort to launch
                                                 Database

                                                 sort()

• pre-Strategy design: conditional logic inside the
  context (Database.sort(policy))
• “policy” (time vs space) is a runtime decision                    TimeIsImportant
• adding a new algorithm means editing                              SpaceIsImportant
  Database.sort() again
• the method grows and becomes harder to maintain

                                           sort(policy){
                                                  if policy==time then MergeSort();
Algorithm selection via conditionals” is          else if policy==space then BubbleSort(); …
the motivation for Strategy                }

Idea: promote “sort” from method to class
• Promote the algorithm into its own object (instead of a branch in a method).   sort()  Implements bubblesort
• Database becomes simpler: it delegates sorting to the strategy object.

But: what kind of parameter does setSortStrategy expects?
• If you pass concrete classes, you still couple the context/client.
• You need a common interface type.

      5/3/2026                                          12 - GoF patterns                                       81
Strategy Pattern                       Strategy is about “composition + delegation”
                                       (Context “has a” Strategy), not “more if/else”.




5/3/2026           12 - GoF patterns                                          82
   Strategy – example 2
How should a Google Maps Clone handle the algorithms to find the
best route?
The algorithm depends on the vehicle. Searching for the best route
for the car is different from searching it for a bike.
Let’s explore a solution without using the Strategy Pattern.
The same “goal” exists (find route), but different
“approaches” exist (car vs bike).
This is exactly the Strategy situation: same
objective, multiple algorithms.
Strategy fits when behavior varies by a policy or
context like vehicle type.

   5/3/2026                                  12 - GoF patterns   83
    Strategy – example 2
 1. Define the Context and the Strategy Interface.
 2. Define the concrete strategies through inheritance.
• Don’t start by writing if/else in the Context.
• Start by identifying:
    • what remains stable (context responsibilities)
    • what varies (strategy behaviors)
• Then implement each behavior variant as its own class.
Strategy is a systematic refactor: isolate varying behavior behind an interface.




                     See strategy1
    5/3/2026         code example            12 - GoF patterns                     84
• Strategy can be pluggable implementations
   of a capability (network connection).                       Strategy – example 3
• The Context (NetworkConnection) delegates
   send/receive to whichever strategy is set.
• The environment (mobile/home/office) can
   drive strategy selection.
                                                                                    Context =
Strategy is broadly applicable whenever you                                   {Mobile, Home, Office}
want to swap implementations at runtime.

      5/3/2026                                  12 - GoF patterns                                      85
     Strategy – wrap up


•     We use the Strategy Pattern when we have different strategies with same goal but
    different approach and use cases.
•     We use the Strategy Pattern when classes differ only by some behaviours.
•     It’s important to think what to pass (and how) to the strategy interface. Different
    strategies may need different data.
•     Using the Strategy leads to a bigger code base. It’s not to be over-used. If
    strategies differ by a small amount, other design pattern may be better.

5/3/2026         12 - GoF patterns   87
Introducing the Composite Pattern
 • Models tree structures that represent part-whole hierarchies with
   arbitrary depth and width.
 • The Composite Pattern lets client treat individual objects and
   compositions of these objects uniformly

 We want the client to call the same operation (e.g., print(), getCost(),
 draw()) on:
 • a Leaf (single item), and
 • a Composite (container of items),
 without caring which one it is.

 “uniform treatment of leaf and composite”
5/3/2026                         12 - GoF patterns                          88
What is common between these definitions?
  • Software System:
        • Definition: A software system consists of subsystems which are either other
          subsystems or collection of classes
        • Composite: Subsystem (A software system consists of subsystems which
          consists of subsystems , which consists of subsystems, which...)
        • Leaf node: Class
  • Software Lifecycle:
        • Definition: The software lifecycle consists of a set of development activities
          which are either other actitivies or collection of tasks
        • Composite: Activity (the software lifecycle consists of activities which
          consist of activities, which consist of activities, which....)
        • Leaf node: Task.

  Riconoscete le situazioni composite nell'analisi: "Le cose contengono
  ricorsivamente cose dello stesso tipo". Se la Vs. struttura è del tipo: "A contiene A
  che contiene A... e infine contiene B", il pattern composite è un buon candidato
 5/3/2026                                12 - GoF patterns                                 89
Component       The “magic” is recursion: composite calls the same operation
                on its children, which may themselves be composites.




                                         • Component (common interface)
                                           defines the operations the client
                                           will use
                                         • Leaf implements operation()
                                           directly (base behavior)
                                         • Composite (holds children)
                                           implements operation() by
                                           delegating to children (recursive
                                           traversal)

     Design Example
• Subsystem 1 can look into the Subsystem 2 (vehicle
  subsystem) and call on any component or class                         Subsystem 1
  operation at will.
• This is “Spaghetti Design”
• Why is this good?
    • Efficiency, no extra layer
• Why is this bad?                                                      Subsystem 2
    • Can’t expect the caller to understand how
      the subsystem works or the complex                         Seat
      relationships within the subsystem.
    • We can be assured that the subsystem                                  Card
      will be misused, leading to non-portable
      code                                                       AIM                  SA/RT
Direct access to subsystem internals = high
coupling, misuse, and change ripple effects

     5/3/2026                                12 - GoF patterns                                97
Subsystem Design with Façade, Adapter,
Bridge
• The ideal structure of a subsystem consists of
      • an interface object
      • a set of application domain objects (entity objects) modeling real entities or
        existing systems
           • Some of the application domain objects are interfaces to existing systems
      • one or more control objects
• We can use design patterns to realize this subsystem structure
• Realization of the Interface Object: Facade
      • Provides the interface to the subsystem
• Interface to existing systems: Adapter or Bridge
      • Provides the interface to existing system (legacy system)
      • The existing system is not necessarily object-oriented

Façade is for how clients access a subsystem. Adapter/Bridge is for how the
subsystem accesses external/legacy components.
5/3/2026                                    12 - GoF patterns                            98
Realizing an Opaque Architecture with a
Facade                     VIP Subsystem
           • The subsystem decides
             exactly how it is accessed
           • No need to worry about
             misuse by callers
           • If a façade is used the
             subsystem can be used in                          Vehicle Subsystem API
             an early integration test
              • We need to write only a driver
                                                               Seat    Card
           The façade gives us a clean
           boundary; internal changes
           stay internal
                                                               AIM            SA/RT

5/3/2026                                   12 - GoF patterns                           99
Facade Pattern




                                     Façade is an entry point
                                     that delegates; it doesn’t
                                     replace the subsystem, it
                                     wraps the subsystem for
                                     simpler use.
  5/3/2026       12 - GoF patterns                    100
 When should you use these Design
 Patterns?
• A façade should be offered by all subsystems in a software system who offer a
  service. Façade = subsystem boundary.
     • The façade delegates requests to the appropriate components within the subsystem. The
       façade usually does not have to be changed, when the components are changed
• The adapter design pattern should be used to interface to existing components.
  Adapter = wrap a legacy API.
     • Example: A smart card software system should use an adapter for a smart card reader
       from a specific manufacturer
• The bridge design pattern should be used to interface to a set of objects. Bridge
  = decouple abstraction from implementations when both can vary.
     • where the full set of objects is not completely known at analysis or design time.
     • when a subsystem or component must be replaced later after the system has been
       deployed and client programs use it in the field.
 5/3/2026                                  12 - GoF patterns                                 101
Façade vs controller
• Controller: who is in charge for managing a UI request (may include
  business logic)

• Façade: how to provide a single proxy class for a subsystem (no
  logic implied): façade’s logic is mostly delegation and/or
  orchestration, not domain rules

• A Controller coordinates a use case flow (system events). A Façade
  offers a stable subsystem API and forwards work to internal
  objects.(They can co-exist: the controller may call the façade.)

• https://stackoverflow.com/questions/839359/front-controller-vs-
  fa%C3%A7ade-pattern/4430089

design” by giving

subsystem, hiding

Adapters behind that
façade to integrate
legacy/external
systems.

(1) Client calls the Façade instead of calling many subsystem objects directly — this is the “single entry point” idea.
(2) Façade delegates to internal subsystem services. The client never needs to know these exist. (3) For payment,
the Façade delegates to PaymentService, which calls a stable interface (PaymentGateway). (4) PaymentGateway
is implemented by LegacyPayAdapter, which translates the request into the external system’s API/protocol call
(e.g., “SOAP over HTTP”) — exactly the “Adapter to existing system”

 Observer Pattern Motivation                                                      Portfolio

• Problem:                                                                                 *
                                                                                       Stock
   • We have an object that changes its state quite often
            • Example: A Portfolio of stocks
   • We want to provide multiple views of the current state of
     the portfolio
            • Example: Histogram view, pie chart view, time line view, alarm
• Requirements:
   • The system should maintain consistency across the
     (redundant) views, whenever the state of the observed
     object changes
   • The system design should be highly extensible
            • It should be possible to add new views without having to recompile the
              observed object or the existing views.
• Observer need: one changing object → many dependent
  objects that must stay consistent.

             Observer Pattern: Decouples an Abstraction from its Views

        5/3/2026                        12 - GoF patterns                        109
Observer Pattern                                             Observer isn’t “one fixed mechanism”;
                                                             you must choose how much information
                                                             is sent in updates (push vs pull).

       • Models a 1-to-many dependency between objects
           • Connects the state of an observed object, the subject with many
             observing objects, the observers
       • Usage:
           • Maintaining consistency across redundant states
           • Optimizing a batch of changes to maintain consistency
       • Three variants for maintaining the consistency:
           • Push Notification: Every time the state of the subject changes, all
             the observers are notified of the change
           • Push-Update Notification: The subject also sends the state that
             has been changed to the observers
           • Pull Notification: Subject notifies observers; observers then pull
             what they need via getState() or similar.
       • Also called Publish and Subscribe.

5/3/2026         12 - GoF patterns   117
Command
The GoF Command pattern is one of the most practical
behavioral patterns because it turns an action/request into an
object, so one can store it, queue it, log it, reuse it, and even
undo it.
Encapsulating a request as an object, we can parameterize
clients with requests, queue/log them.
Instead of calling receiver.doSomething() directly from
UI/voice/menu code, we create a Command object that
represents “doSomething”, and pass that around.

5/3/2026                      12 - GoF patterns                     118
                                                           Alexa
Command - example                                    someMethod()


Alexa invokes operations on a switch.


This could result in a set of conditional
                                                          Switch
if statements in Alexa: …
                                                         turnOn()
                                                         turnOff()
if (RequestType==turnOn){
     switch.turnOn()
} else…

5/3/2026                         12 - GoF patterns                   119
Command Pattern
• We want to make “command” a first-class object (not just a method)
• e.g., to build a user interface , to provide menus
• We want to make the menus reusable across many applications
      • The applications only know what has to be done when a command from the
        menu is selected
      • We don’t want to hardcode the menu commands for the various applications
• Such a user interface can easily be implemented with the Command
  Pattern.
• If “command” is an object, you can easily manipulate, queue, undo…


5/3/2026                             12 - GoF patterns                         120
Command
An application may depend on Invoker
objects that invokes operations on different                                           Invoker
Receiver objects. Invokers and Receivers are
closely tied to each other


This could result in a set of conditional if                                         Receiver
statements in the invoker: …
                                                                                    action1()
                                                                                    action2()
if (RequestType=TypeA){
     receiver.action1()
}                                                           Without Command, the invoker becomes a
                                                            “switchboard” full of `if/else`, tightly coupled
                                                            to many receivers and operations.
5/3/2026                                12 - GoF patterns                                         121
      Command pattern
    Class Invoker {                                                  Command

                                  Invoker

    someMethod(){}
    …
    }

                                                                ConcreteCommand1
• Define a Command abstraction with an execute()
  operation.                                                       execute()
• Create multiple ConcreteCommand classes
  implementing execute() (e.g., ConcreteCommand1,                   ConcreteCommand2
  ConcreteCommand2).
• The Invoker stores a Command reference.                               execute()
• The Invoker no longer needs `if/else` to decide
  actions, it just calls: commandX.execute();

      5/3/2026                              12 - GoF patterns                          122
Command
The Command pattern suggests creating an abstraction for the
processing to be carried out in response to client requests.
Declare a common interface to be implemented by different
concrete implementers (“ConcreteCommand”).
Command objects are not the real implementation: they make
use of Receiver objects in offering this functionality

A Command is mainly a wrapper around a receiver call (or a
small sequence of receiver calls), turning “a method call” into “a
first-class object.”

5/3/2026                      12 - GoF patterns                      123
 Command pattern
                                                                                      object
                              Receiver
                                                      «binds»   ConcreteCommand1

                              action1()                            execute()
                                                      «binds»
                              action2()
                                                                    ConcreteCommand2
                     method
                                                                        execute()



• A command is a method turned into an object (see labels method / object)
• ConcreteCommand1 is bound to Receiver.action1()
• ConcreteCommand2 is bound to Receiver.action2()
Each command represents one user-visible action and knows which receiver
and which receiver method(s) to call.
5/3/2026                          12 - GoF patterns                                 124
Command pattern
                                                                                Command
                                           Invoker
                                                                               execute()
                 Client


                                        Receiver

                                        action1()                             execute()
                                                                 «binds»
                                        action2()
                                                                               ConcreteCommand2

 Full collaboration: Client, Invoker, Commands, Receiver                           execute()
 (the complete GoF “cast”)
 The Client wires everything together (creates commands,
 binds them to receivers, gives them to invokers). After that,
 the invoker only needs to trigger execute().

5/3/2026                                  12 - GoF patterns                                125
   Command pattern
                                                                                            Command
                                              Invoker
                                                                                          execute()
               Client

                                                                                                                      object
                                          Receiver
                                                                      «binds»      ConcreteCommand1

                                         action1()                                       execute()
                                                                        «binds»
                                         action2()
                                                                                            ConcreteCommand2
                              method
                                                                                                  execute()
Who does what (responsibility split)
• Client (in this case a user interface builder) creates a ConcreteCommand and binds it to an action operation in Receiver
• Client hands the ConcreteCommand over to the Invoker which stores it (for example in a menu)
• The Invoker has the responsibility to execute or undo a command (based on a string entered by the user)

    5/3/2026                                              12 - GoF patterns                                                  126
Command pattern: roles + responsibilities
• The Command interface: This is usually just a single method
  for executing the command.
• The ConcreteCommand: This has an “execute” operation with
  parameters that passes the call to the receiver. In the classic
  approach, a command only invokes one or more methods of
  a Receiver rather than perform business logic.
• The Receiver: knows how to perform the action.
• The Invoker: keeps the logic for managing commands.
• The Client: creates a ConcreteCommand object and sets
  the Receiver.

5/3/2026                     12 - GoF patterns                  127
Command pattern
• The Command pattern can be used together with the following
  options:
      • adding commands to a queue to execute them later;
      • supporting undo/redo operations;
      • storing a history of commands;
      • serializing commands to store them on a disk;
• These options (governed by Invoker) are not essential to the
  pattern but are often used in practice.
Once requests are objects, you can treat them like data: store,
replay, undo, schedule.

5/3/2026                          12 - GoF patterns               128
Command pattern
Client binds → Invoker triggers → Command delegates → Receiver executes.




 5/3/2026                       12 - GoF patterns                    129
Decouples boundary objects
from control objects
 • The command pattern can be nicely used to decouple
   boundary objects from control objects:
       • Boundary objects such as menu items and buttons, send
         messages to the command objects (I.e. the control objects)
       • Only the command objects modify entity objects
 • When the user interface is changed (for example, a menu
   bar is replaced by a tool bar), only the boundary objects are
   modified.

 Command helps isolate UI changes from business/control
 logic: great for maintainable UI systems.
5/3/2026                            12 - GoF patterns                 130
Command Pattern Applicability
• Parameterize clients with different requests
• Queue or log requests
• Support undoable operations

• Uses:
      • Undo queues
      • Database transaction buffering

If we need undo/logging/queuing, Command is a natural fit.

                           • Write code to create two concrete commands

                           (and bind it), rather than rewriting invoker conditionals.

  • GoF patterns provides useful templates to solve recurrent situations.

esempio di metodo
pro e contro
pro
-   lazy initialization

        la classe viene istanziata solo quando viene chiamata.

-   incapsulamento

         possibilita’ di nascondere dettagli di implementazione del codice dall’user.

-   flessiblita’

         possibilita’ di cambiare il comportamento di getInstance() senza andare a
         modificare il codice chiamante.
contro

-   thread safety

        se non gestito, possibile caso in cui due thread creano l’istanza
    contemporaneamente. (vengono istanziate 2 o piu’ banche invece che una)

-   complessita’
-   scalabilita’

         esempio: essendo l’istanza singleton unica, potrebbe rappresentare un
         collo di bottiglia nel caso piu’ parti del sistema richiedano l’accesso alla
         risorsa contemporaneamente. (una banca gestisce tutte le transazioni)
RECAP
recap
-   il singleton si assicura che esista una singola istanza della classe in
    ogni dato momento.
-   vi si accede tramite un metodo public static getInstance()
-   equivalente all’inizializzazione `static` di una classe, ma aggiunge
    incapsulamento, flessibilita’ e thread safety.
Strategy
Used to define a family of algorithms,
encapsulate each one, and make
them interchangeable.
Strategy lets the algorithms vary
independently from clients that use it.
Motivation

Often we use different algorithms with the same goal but a different approach.

Using the Strategy pattern we implement these different algorithms with the
same interface, doing so:
 ● We can more easily add new strategies.
 ● Decreases problems occurring when changing existing strategies.
 ● Removes if cascades.
Example
Google Maps clone

How should a Google Maps Clone handle the algorithms to find the best route?

The algorithm depends on the vehicle. Searching for the best route for the car
is different from searching it for a bike.

Let’s explore a solution without using
the Strategy Pattern.
What if we want to add public transport?
Now, let’s implement
Strategy

1. Define the Context and the Strategy Interface.
2. Define the concrete strategies through inheritance.
Concrete strategies implement the virtual function of the interface.
The Vehicle class is not the context in this case. It’s just another
step between context and strategy.
The Navigator is the context in this case, because it decides the
data to pass to the strategy.
We can define various vehicles, with the same or different
strategies. The current vehicle in the navigator determines the
strategy to use to calculate the fastest path.
Wrap up

● We use the Strategy Pattern when we have different strategies with same goal
  but different approach and use cases.
● We use the Strategy Pattern when classes differ only by some behaviours.
● It’s important to think what to pass (and how) to the strategy interface. Different
  strategies may need different data.
● Using the Strategy leads to a bigger code base. It’s not to be over-used. If
  strategies differ by a small amount, other design pattern may be better.
GRASP

19/04/2026                          10 - System modeling   2
 Responsibility-Driven Design (RDD)
  After identifying your requirements and creating a domain model, then
  add methods to the software classes, and define the messaging
  between the objects to fulfill the requirements.
  • Detailed object design is usually done from the point of
    view of the metaphor of:
        • Objects have responsibilities
        • Objects collaborate
  • Responsibilities are an abstraction.
        • The responsibility for persistence.
             • Large-grained responsibility.
        • The responsibility for the sales tax calculation.
             • More fine-grained responsibility.


19/04/2026                                  Lecture 11 - GRASP            3
 Responsibility-Driven Design (RDD)
  • The UML defines a responsibility as "a contract or obligation
    of a classifier".

  • Responsibilities are related to the obligations of an object in
    terms of its behavior.

  Basically, these responsibilities are of the following two types:

  • knowing
  • doing


19/04/2026                       Lecture 11 - GRASP                   4
Responsibility-driven design




19/04/2026        Lecture 11 - GRASP   5
GRASP
             General Responsibility Assignment Software Patterns

             Recognize that according to Craig Larman:
             1. “The skillful assignment of responsibilities is
                extremely important in object design,
             2. Determining the assignment of responsibilities often
                occurs during the creation of interaction diagrams
                and certainly during programming.”


19/04/2026                          Lecture 11 - GRASP                 6
   GRASP
UML case: visual modeling that allows us to explore visually

Drawing and coding activity: we apply OO design principles based on the
metaphor of responsibility-driven design, assigning responsibilities to objects
that shall collaborate (responsibilities, roles and collaboration)

• use case text: visible behavior of objects realizing the use case
• system sequence diagrams: system operation messages
• operation contracts: achievements of the sw objects in system operations
  (post conditions)




19/04/2026                           Lecture 11 - GRASP                           7
   GRASP
A Design Model may have
    •hundreds or even thousands of software classes and
    •hundreds or thousands of responsibilities to be assigned.


During object design, when interactions between
objects are defined; we make choices about the
assignment of responsibilities to software classes.



19/04/2026                        Lecture 11 - GRASP             8
   GRASP
The translation of responsibilities into classes and methods is
influenced by the granularity of the responsibility.

The responsibility to "provide access to relational databases"
may involve dozens of classes and hundreds of methods,
packaged in a subsystem.

By contrast, the responsibility to "create a Sale" may involve
only one or few methods.

A responsibility is not the same thing as a method, but methods
are implemented to fulfill responsibilities. Responsibilities are
implemented using methods that either act alone or collaborate
with other methods and objects.

19/04/2026                     Lecture 11 - GRASP                   9
   GRASP
A pattern is a named problem/solution pair that can be
applied in new context, with advice on how to apply it in
novel situations and discussion of its trade-offs.

Naming a pattern, technique, or principle has the following
advantages:
• it supports chunking and incorporating that concept into
  our understanding and memory.
• it facilitates communication.

19/04/2026                          10 - System modeling   12
Creator
• Problem: Who creates A?
• Solution: assign class B the responsibility to create an instance
  of A if one or more are true:
     • B contains or aggregates A
     • B records A
     • B closely uses A
     • B has the initializing data for A

• If more than one option applies, prefer a class B which
  aggregates or contains class A

19/04/2026                                                       Lecture 11 - GRASP                         14
Creator – Sales aggregates
SalesLineItems
    •Note that ‘Sales’ aggregates, uses, etc. SalesLineItems.
    •Sales contains / has_a (one or more) SalesLineItems.

    •Since a Sale contains many SalesLineItem objects, the Creator pattern
    suggests that Sale is a good candidate to have the responsibility of
    creating SalesLineItem objects.

    •This may sound ‘all too obvious.’ But how many times have you been
    aware that you needed to create an array of objects and been uncertain
    exactly where or in which object to create this array?

             The sequence diagram suggests an assignment of responsibilities that requires that a makeLineItem
             method be defined in Sale. (This is an additional ‘responsibility’ in Sale)

             Once again, the context for these decisions was while drawing the interaction diagram.

19/04/2026                                                       Lecture 11 - GRASP                              16
Benefits of Creator Pattern:
   •we want a ‘creator’ that needs to be connected to the
   ‘created’ object;

   •Please note that sometimes a creator is found by looking
   for the class that has the initializing data needed to be
   passed during creation.

   •The use of initializing data that suggests a class as a
   Creator is actually an example of the Expert pattern.
   Initializing data might be ‘passed in’ during creation via
   some kind of initialization method, like a Constructor, or, ….

Contraindications or caveats
• Creation may require significant complexity:
     • recycling instances for performance reasons
     • conditionally creating instances from a family of similar classes
• In these instances, other patterns are available – e.g. Factory

    Expert
    Name: Information Expert
    Problem: How to assign responsibilities to objects?
    Solution: Assign responsibility to the class that has
     the information needed to fulfill it




19/04/2026                     Lecture 11 - GRASP           25
Expert
• “… expresses the common ‘intuition’ that objects do things
  related to the information they have.”
• Assign the responsibility for “knowing” [something] to the object
  (class) that has the information necessary to fulfill the
  responsibility.

•Domain Model contains conceptual classes of the real-world domain; These are often

responsibilities)
•Design Model contains the software classes (These will have attributes, methods,
responsibilities)


•First choice: If we already have relevant classes in Design Model, choose this first.
•Second choice: look into Domain Model and attempt to use (or expand) its
      representations to inspire the creation of corresponding design classes.

19/04/2026                                         Lecture 11 - GRASP   42
    EXPERT
     • Information Expert is a frequently used Design Pattern which has great value in
       assigning responsibilities.

     • It is the guiding principle; continuously used in object design.

     • Used extensively in data structures class.

     • Note that the fulfillment of responsibilities often requires spanning several different
       classes.
             • This implies that there are several partial information experts who collaborate in the task.


     • Fundamentally, “information experts” do things relative to the information they ‘know.’


19/04/2026                                         Lecture 11 - GRASP                                         43
EXPERT
• Who should be responsible for saving Sale in a database?

• Clearly, much of the information is ‘in’ the Sale object and thus by
  ‘Expert’ an argument could be made to put that responsibility in the
  Sale class.

• But, by extension, then, EACH class would have its own services to
  save itself in the database.

• This is untenable and leads to problems in cohesion and coupling,
  reuse, and duplication
19/04/2026                    Lecture 11 - GRASP                         44
EXPERT
Cohesion and Coupling – Two Essential Design Principles:


• Sale would have to now contain logic related to database handling, such as related to
  SQL and JDBC (for J2EE) or more
• If we included logic to save the data, the class would now no longer be focused
  (decreased cohesion!) on just pure application of logic of ‘being a sale;”
• Class now has other kinds of responsibilities, like ‘saving itself’ which lowers its cohesion!
  This is NOT desirable
• Rule of Thumb: We always want to separate I/O from computations / data manipulation )




19/04/2026                                 Lecture 11 - GRASP                                      45
 EXPERT

Cohesion and Coupling – Two Essential Design Principles - more:
• Classes like this one (and other classes that need database services) need to be
  coupled to the technical database services – likely found of another subsystem, such
  as JDBC services, rather than ‘just’ being coupled to other objects in the domain layer
  of software objects.
• This, of course makes the coupling tighter (in general not desirable) – but this
  provides the benefit of increasing its cohesion (highly desirable) and strengthens the
  case for its possible reuse..
• (And it is likely that similar database logic would have to be duplicated in many
  persistent classes where this responsibility relegated to the classes themselves)


 19/04/2026                               Lecture 11 - GRASP                                46
 EXPERT

Keep application logic in one place (like domain objects)
Generally found in an ‘application layer’ if your architectural pattern is a layered architecture.


Keep database logic in another place (e.g. persistence services subsystem), rather than
intermingling different system concerns in same component.
 Supporting a separation of major concerns improves coupling and cohesion in a design.


Thus, even though “by Expert” - could be justification to putting responsibility for database
services into the Sale class, for other reasons, (usually coupling and cohesion), this
represents a very poor design choice.



 19/04/2026                                  Lecture 11 - GRASP                                      47
Benefits of Expert:
• Information encapsulation is maintained, since objects use
  their own information to fulfill tasks.
• This usually supports low coupling, which leads to a more
  robust and maintainable system. (‘Low Coupling’ is also a
  GRASP pattern).

Behavior is distributed across the classes that have the
required information thus encouraging more cohesive,
‘lightweight’ class definitions that are easier to understand
and maintain.

19/04/2026                          10 - System modeling   49
  CONTROLLER
Problem: What object in the domain (or application coordination layer)
 receives requests for work from the UI layer?

System operations (see SSD): major input events to the system

The controller is the first object (beyond the UI layer, if any) that is
 responsible for receiving or handling a system operations message.

Note that UI objects delegate system operation request to a controller.


19/04/2026                        Lecture 11 - GRASP                       50
Controller Pattern: System Events
   Most applications have ‘System Events.’                                                        System

   Typically the ‘system’ is modeled as a class during                                        endSale()
                                                                                              enterItem()

   analysis                                                                                   makeNewSale()
                                                                                              makePayment()
                                                                                              …




    Do not infer that there will be a class named System in Design.
    Rather, during Design, a Controller class is assigned the responsibilities for
    system operations.

             Remember who is developing requirements and performing analysis…..
             We simply do not know what the implementation (solution) will be at this time.



19/04/2026                                           Lecture 11 - GRASP                                       51
19/04/2026   Lecture 11 - GRASP   52
CONTROLLER
 Solution: Assign the responsibility to a class representing one of the
     following choices:
 •   A façade controller, which represents
       1.    the overall system
       2.    A root object
       3.    A device that the software is running within, or
       4.    A major subsystem
 •       A use case or session controller which represents a use case
         scenario in which the system operation occurs. This is normally
         named
             <usecasename> Handler,
             <UseCaseName> Coordinator or
             <UseCaseName> Session )


19/04/2026                                  Lecture 11 - GRASP             54
Controller
  • The UI layer gets data and an “Enter Item” message (or
    some other message, in the case of the assignment)




19/04/2026                  Lecture 11 - GRASP               55
Façade Controller
• Suitable when there are not too many system events
• This could be an abstraction of the overall physical unit, such as
  PhoneSwitch, CashRegister, etc.




19/04/2026                   Lecture 11 - GRASP                    56
Use Case Controller
• When using a façade controller leads to low cohesion and high
  coupling
• When the code in façade controller gets too big




19/04/2026                 Lecture 11 - GRASP                     57
Bloated Controller
• There is only one and it handles too many events
• The controller performs the work rather than delegating it
• Controller has many attributes and maintains significant system
  or domain info




19/04/2026                  Lecture 11 - GRASP                  58
Controller
 • A Controller is a non-user interface object responsible for receiving or handling a
   system event.

 • A Controller may represent a receiver of a signal or a handler of all system events
   in a use case scenario.

 • Input events might come from
         •   a GUI operated by a person, or
         •   a call from a telecommunications switch, or
         •   a signal from a sensor, etc.

 (Note that classes such as window, applet, widget, view, document, etc. are not used. These
 kinds of classes do not fulfill the tasks associated with system events; rather, these typically
 receive events and delegate them to some kind of controller.)

19/04/2026                              Lecture 11 - GRASP                                               60
Controller - Discussion

• In all cases, some kind of ‘Handler’ for these events must be chosen.

• It is this Controller Pattern that provides some guidance for generally acceptable
  suitable choices.

• Again, a controller class is often some kind of façade into the domain
  (application) layer from the interface (boundary) layer.

• Often, we have a single controller for an entire use case,

• If so, then the state of the use case is maintained in the Controller.



19/04/2026                                Lecture 11 - GRASP                           61
CONTROLLER
• We want to be careful to NOT give Controllers too much
  responsibility.

• Controller classes delegate work to other objects;

• They coordinate / control the activity, but NOT do too much of
  the work itself other than coordinating and sequencing.

• But remember, the Controller ‘knows’ where the Model is and
  what is available to satisfy the system events.

                                     Typically the controller
                                      keeps the state or
                                     uses a “Model”

19/04/2026                          10 - System modeling   69
    LOW COUPLING

      • Name: Low Coupling
      • Problem: How to reduce the impact of change
        and encourage reuse?
      • Solution: Assign a responsibility so that coupling
        (linking classes) remains low.
             COUPLING: Measures the degree of dependency that exists between
             modules.



19/04/2026                                Lecture 11 - GRASP                   70
Low Coupling
• Coupling is a measure of how strongly one element is
  connected to, has knowledge of, or relies upon other elements
• An element with Low Coupling is not dependent upon too many
  other elements. But how many is too many?
• High coupling is not really the problem; it is high coupling to
  potentially unstable (likely to be changed) objects.




19/04/2026                  Lecture 11 - GRASP                  71
LOW Coupling
Examples of coupling:
     A has a method A.getB() which returns a B object

     A has a method A.m( B b, …) which has a B object as input

     A has a method A.m() which internally uses a method from B

     A calls C.m(B b …) which expects a B object

     A has an attribute of type B

     ...

19/04/2026                          Lecture 11 - GRASP            72
Low Coupling
• Problems with high coupling:
     • Forced local changes because of changes in related classes
     • Harder to understand in isolation
     • Harder to reuse because it requires other classes
• Assign responsibility so coupling remains low
• This reduces the impact of change




19/04/2026                       Lecture 11 - GRASP                 73
LOW COUPLING: NextGen example

  • If we need to create a Payment
    instance and associate it with
    Sale, who should do it?




19/04/2026                  Lecture 11 - GRASP   74
LOW COUPLING: NextGen example
Since Register records a payment, the Creator pattern suggests it

                           : Register                                    : Sale


               makePayment()
                                        create()
                                                           p : Payment



                                          addPayment( p )




    This design however adds coupling of Register to Payment;
19/04/2026                                  Lecture 11 - GRASP                    75
LOW COUPLING: NextGen example
  On the other hand, Sale could create Payment
                             : Register                : Sale


                makePayment()
                                     makePayment()
                                                                create()   : Payment




  This design does not increase the coupling, so it is to be preferred

19/04/2026                                Lecture 11 - GRASP                           76
LOW COUPLING
• Understandability: Classes are easier to understand in isolation
• Maintainability: Classes aren’t affected by changes in other
  components
• Reusability: easier to grab hold of classes
But:
• Don’t sweat coupling to stable classes (in libraries or pervasive,
  well-tested classes)

     Responsibility Driven Design

19/04/2026                          10 - System modeling   79
    HIGH COHESION
      • Name: High Cohesion
      • Problem: How to keep objects focused,
        understandable, and manageable, and as a side
        effect, support Low Coupling?

      • Solution: Assign a responsibility so that cohesion
        remains high. Use this to evaluate alternatives.



19/04/2026                        Lecture 11 - GRASP         80
High Cohesion
• Cohesion is a measure of how strongly related the
  responsibilities of an element are
• A class with low cohesion does many unrelated things. It is
  hard to understand, hard to reuse, hard to maintain, and
  delicate
• In the POS system, if Register creates the payment, this is less
  cohesive than if Sale does it




19/04/2026                   Lecture 11 - GRASP                      81
High Cohesion
• A class with high cohesion has a relatively small number of
  highly-related methods
• It collaborates with other objects
• Modular design




19/04/2026                  Lecture 11 - GRASP                  82
High Cohesion: NextGen example

  • If we need to create a Payment
    instance and associate it with
    Sale, who should do it?




19/04/2026                  Lecture 11 - GRASP   83
High Cohesion: NextGen example

  • Since Register records a payment,            : Register                                          : Sale


  • the Creator pattern suggests it makePayment()
                                                                        create()
                                                                                       p : Payment



                                                                          addPayment( p )




this is acceptable; but if we make the Register class responsible for additional work, it
will become increasingly burdened it would become a "bloated" incohesive object.

The Payment creation does not decrease cohesion, but as part of a larger picture of
overall responsibility assignment, it may suggest a trend toward low cohesion.

19/04/2026                                   Lecture 11 - GRASP                                          84
High Cohesion: NextGen example
  • On the other hand, Sale could create Payment
                            : Register                : Sale


                makePayment()
                                    makePayment()
                                                               create()   : Payment




  By high cohesion, this design is again preferrable

19/04/2026                               Lecture 11 - GRASP                           85
High Cohesion




19/04/2026      Lecture 11 - GRASP   86
  High Cohesion
• Understandability, maintainability
• Complements Low Coupling

But:
• Avoid grouping of responsibilities or code into one class or
  component to simplify maintenance by one person. Why?
• Sometimes desirable to create less cohesive server
  objects that provide an interface for many operations, due
  to performance needs associated with remote objects and
  remote communication

19/04/2026                          10 - System modeling   88
  Pure Fabrication

 Problem: Existing objects, ie domain objects, are not
  appropriate to have the responsibility
       Solution suggested by Information Expert not appropriate
       Might violate high cohesion, low coupling


 Solution: Fabricate (ie create, make up) a new object to hold
  the responsibility


19/04/2026                         Lecture 11 - GRASP             89
19/04/2026   Lecture 11 - GRASP   90
Pure Fabrication
• Assign a highly cohesive set of responsibilities to an artificial or
  convenience class that does not represent a problem domain
  concept—something made up, to support high cohesion, low
  coupling, and reuse.




19/04/2026                    Lecture 11 - GRASP                         91
Pure Fabrication

  Design of objects
        • Representational decomposition. (typically, from domain design)
        • Behavorial decomposition (new)
  It’s OK for OO software to have objects representing
    behaviour, ie use case, process, function, strategy, TS
        But do not overdo it

  All GoF design patterns are Pure Fabrications

19/04/2026                          Lecture 11 - GRASP                      92
Topics
     Polymorphism

     Protected Variations

     Indirection




19/04/2026                  10 - System modeling   93
  Polymorphism
Problem: Who is responsible when behavior varies by type?

Solution: When the behavior varies depending on the type of
  an object, assign the responsibility for that behavior to the
  object’s type using polymorphic operations.
In other words: don’t ask “what type are you?” — just call a
  method and let the right implementation run.



19/04/2026                   Lecture 11 - GRASP                   94
  Polymorphism
So rather than:
• if (paymentMethod == CARD) ...
• switch(memberType) ...
• if (shipping == EXPRESS) ...

You do:
• paymentMethod.authorize(...)
• loanPolicy.dueDateFor(...)
• shippingPolicy.costFor(...)

19/04/2026                 Lecture 11 - GRASP   95
    Polymorphism
public interface LoanPolicy {
    LocalDate dueDateFor(Member member, Copy copy, LocalDate checkoutDate);
}


public final class StandardLoanPolicy implements LoanPolicy {
    @Override public LocalDate dueDateFor(Member member, Copy copy, LocalDate checkoutDate) {
        return checkoutDate.plusDays(21);
    }
}


public final class StudentLoanPolicy implements LoanPolicy {
    @Override public LocalDate dueDateFor(Member member, Copy copy, LocalDate checkoutDate) {
        return checkoutDate.plusDays(28);
    }
}


Then the use case stays stable:
LocalDate due = loanPolicy.dueDateFor(member, copy, today);


19/04/2026                                         Lecture 11 - GRASP                           96
  Protected variations
Problem: How to assign responsibilities to objects, subsystems,
 and systems so that the variations or instability in these
 elements do not have an undesirable impact on other
 elements?

Solution: Identify the parts of the system that are likely to
 change (the “variations”) and “protect” the rest of the design
 from those changes by introducing a stable interface/barrier.
 In practice: put a “stable seam” between code that changes
 often and code that you want to keep stable.


19/04/2026                   Lecture 11 - GRASP                   97
  Protected variations
• Step A: Identify likely change points
     • Ask: “What is most likely to change next quarter?
     • ”Examples: “We may swap payment provider” or “Fine calculation
       changes by country” or “Shipping rules change per service level”
• Step B: Create a stable abstraction (interface/contract)
     • Define what the stable part needs, not what the unstable
       implementation offers.
• Step C: Put volatile details behind the abstraction
     • Implement “adapters/strategies” that hide SDK calls, DB queries,
       conditional rules, etc.
• Step D: Inject/select the variant without changing callers
     • Use constructor injection (plain Java), a factory, or a selector.
19/04/2026                          Lecture 11 - GRASP                     98
   Protected variations
public interface PaymentGateway {
  PaymentReceipt charge(PaymentRequest request);
}

public final class StripePaymentGateway implements PaymentGateway {
  @Override public PaymentReceipt charge(PaymentRequest request) {
    // Stripe SDK details stay here
    return new PaymentReceipt(true, "tx_123");
  }
}

 19/04/2026                  Lecture 11 - GRASP                  99
  Indirection
Problem: How to assign responsibilities to avoid direct
 coupling?

Solution: Assign responsibility to an intermediate object to
 decouple two components that would otherwise be directly
 connected. In plain terms: insert a “middleman” so that
 component A doesn’t need to know (or depend on)
 component B.




19/04/2026                  Lecture 11 - GRASP                 100
  Indirection
1) “A knows too much about B”
     • Example: a use case calls an email provider SDK directly.
2) “Changes ripple through the system”
     • Adding a new notification channel forces edits in multiple places.
3) “Hard dependencies block testing”
     • Unit tests require DB, network, or SDK credentials.
4) “Feature growth is mostly add-ons”
     • You keep adding new consumers of the same event (“after checkout,
       also do analytics, fraud check, CRM sync…”).



19/04/2026                         Lecture 11 - GRASP                       101
  quick map
Polymorphism: varies behavior by swapping implementations
at the same interface

Protected Variations: identifies change points and shields them
behind stable interfaces

Indirection: is a mechanism to reduce direct connections —
often used to implement Protected Variations, and sometimes
alongside Polymorphism.

19/04/2026                                         Lecture 11 - GRASP                103
GRASP:
summary
GRASP: summary
• Creator: Assign the responsibility of creating instances of a class to the
  class that has the most knowledge about when and how to create them.

• Information Expert: Assign a responsibility to the class that has the
  necessary information to fulfill it, promoting high cohesion and minimizing
  coupling.

• Low Coupling: Aim for classes to have minimal dependencies on each
  other, facilitating easier maintenance and flexibility in the system.

• High Cohesion: Ensure that the responsibilities within a class are closely
  related and focused, enhancing readability, maintainability, and reusability.

• Indirection: Put a “stable seam” between code that changes often and code
  that you want to keep stable
19/04/2026                        Lecture 11 - GRASP                           105
GRASP: summary
• Controller: Assign the responsibility of handling system events or
  coordinating activities to a controller class, promoting centralized control
  and avoiding cluttered classes.

• Pure Fabrication: Introduce new classes to fulfill responsibilities without
  violating cohesion and coupling principles, promoting cleaner and more
  maintainable designs.

• Indirection: Use intermediaries or abstractions to decouple classes and
  promote flexibility in design.

• Polymorphism: Utilize inheritance and interfaces to enable multiple
  implementations of behaviors, allowing for flexible and extensible systems.

expert should be considered

• Current round count

  19/04/2026                           Lecture 11 - GRASP   110
                         Who takes a turn ?
Doing responsibility  Information expert should be considered
• Current location of the player (starting point of a move): a Piece knows its
   Square and a Player knows its Piece  a Player by LRG
• The two Die objects (to roll them): MonopolyGame is a candidate
   considering the dice being part of the game
• All the squares (to perform a correct move): by LRG, Board

Three partial information experts: is there a dominant information expert?
No, each one has about 1/3 of information
Consider the coupling and cohesion impacts
MonopolyGame can be discarded, it is already doing some work, more work
would impact its cohesion.
Two candidates remain: consider probable future evolutions  buying a
property  Player is the best candidate to be chosen

takeTurn message is sent to each player
1. calculating a random number 2..12
   • Die object with facevalue attribute
2. calculating the new square location
   • Board object knows all its Squares (by LRG)
3. moving the player’s piece from old to new location
   • by LRG, a Player knows its Piece, a Piece knows its Square location
      then by Expert a Piece can set its new location (or receive this new
      location from the Player that’s its owner)

Who can coordinate all this?
Since the Player is responsible for taking a turn, the Player shall coordinate
The player shall have a reference to Die, Board an Piece objects

Library Management Example
  Domain model — “what exists in the business”

  Key domain concepts

  • Book: bibliographic record (ISBN, title, authors)
  • Copy: a physical copy (barcode), can be available/on-
    loan/lost
  • Member: library user (status, limits)
  • Loan: the act of lending one copy to one member for a period
  • Hold: reservation for a book (queue)
  • Fine: money owed due to overdue/lost
