19/04/2026                 Lecture 11 - GRASP                119
Library Management Example
  GRASP usage: mapping patterns to concrete responsibilities
  Practical GRASP assignments for library management

  Information Expert
  Principle: assign behavior to the class that has the required
  information.
  Examples
  • Loan is expert on whether it’s overdue (it knows dueDate
     and returnedAt).
  • Member is expert on whether they can borrow more (it
     knows active loans & limit).
  • Copy is expert on whether it is available.
19/04/2026                   Lecture 11 - GRASP                   120
Library Management Example
  Creator
  Principle: who creates Loan? Often the object that
  aggregates/uses it heavily.

  Good choices:
  • An application service (use-case controller) creates the
    Loan because it orchestrates persistence and policies.
  • Alternatively, a domain factory method
    LoanFactory.createLoan(...).

  LoanFactory can be Pure Fabrication too

19/04/2026                   Lecture 11 - GRASP                121
Library Management Example
  Controller (Application Service)
  Principle: assign system event handling to a controller object.

  Use case: “Checkout Copy”
  • Validate member
  • Check copy availability
  • Enforce holds
  • Create loan
  • Persist updates
  • Publish event/notify


19/04/2026                    Lecture 11 - GRASP                    122
Library Management Example
  Controller does orchestration; domain objects do local rules.

Library Management Example
  Controller does orchestration; domain objects do local rules.




19/04/2026                   Lecture 11 - GRASP                   124
Library Management Example
  Low-coupling
  Principle: reduce dependencies.

  • CheckoutService depends on CopyRepository interface, not
    JDBC implementation.
  • NotificationPort interface prevents coupling to email
    provider.




19/04/2026                  Lecture 11 - GRASP                 125
Library Management Example

  High Cohesion
  Principle: keep classes focused.

  • FinePolicy handles fine calculation (not ReturnService, not
    Loan).
  • NotificationPort handles messaging (not domain model).
  • Domain entities focus on invariants (availability, status
    transitions).



19/04/2026                   Lecture 11 - GRASP                   126
Library Management Example
  Pure Fabrication
  Principle: create a non-domain class to achieve
  cohesion/coupling goals.

  Good pure fabrications :
  • CheckoutService, ReturnService (application services)
  • Repositories (LoanRepository, etc.)
  • FinePolicy, LoanPolicyNotificationPort
    adapterCatalogSearchService that queries read models

  They don’t represent “things” in the library, but they keep
  design clean.

Automated Unit Testing in Java with
             JUnit

          luca boldrin – luigi rizzo

                     17 - JUnit
What is Unit Testing?
• A procedure to validate individual units of Source Code
• Example: A procedure, method or class
• Validating each individual piece reduces errors when integrating the
  pieces together later
Unit tests should usually be:
• fast,
• isolated,
• repeatable,
• automated,
• independent from external systems where possible.

                                 17 - JUnit
Method → Unit Test → Expected Result
In JUnit 5, a typical unit test follows the Arrange-Act-Assert pattern to verify
that a specific method (the unit) behaves as expected.
1. The Method (Class Under Test). This is the actual logic we want to verify.
public class Calculator {
   public int add(int a, int b) {
     return a + b;
   }
}
2. The test class is typically located in src/test/java. You use the @Test
annotation from the JUnit Jupiter API to mark a method as a test case.

                                      17 - JUnit
Method → Unit Test → Expected Result
import org.junit.jupiter.api.Test;
import static org.junit.jupiter.api.Assertions.assertEquals;
class CalculatorTest {
   @Test
    void addition_shouldReturnSumOfTwoNumbers() {
      // 1. Arrange: Set up the object and data
        Calculator calculator = new Calculator();
        // 2. Act: Call the method being tested
        int actualResult = calculator.add(2, 3);
        // 3. Assert: Compare actual result with expected result
        assertEquals(5, actualResult, "The sum of 2 and 3 should be 5");
    }
}
                                         17 - JUnit
Method → Unit Test → Expected Result

3. Expected Result & Outcomes
The Expected Result is the value you define manually based on
requirements (in this case, 5).
• Pass: If actualResult matches 5, the JUnit Test Runner displays a green
  success indicator.
• Fail: If the method returned a different value (e.g., 6), the test fails, and
  JUnit provides an error message showing the discrepancy.




                                      17 - JUnit
Automated Unit Tests
• Allows you to write unit tests in such a way that you can run it
  automatically at every code change
• Automated testing enables running and rerunning tests very
  easily and quickly

• Junit is a unit testing framework for Java which offers a simple
  interface

• “In professional projects, tests are often executed automatically
  by Maven, Gradle, GitHub Actions, Azure DevOps, Jenkins, or
  other CI/CD tools.”
                                17 - JUnit
Topics

Unit testing
Junit overview

Junit test cases
Junit test methods
Junit test suites
Junit from IDE




                     17 - JUnit
Introduction
• JUnit is an open source Java testing framework used to
  write and run repeatable tests

• JUnit is integrated with several IDEs, including Eclipse

• Latest release: check https://junit.org or the official
  GitHub releases page.




                                 17 - JUnit
JUnit 5 Maven dependency
<dependency>
  <groupId>org.junit.jupiter</groupId>
  <artifactId>junit-jupiter</artifactId>
  <version>5.14.4</version>
  <scope>test</scope>
</dependency>




                                     17 - JUnit
An example unit test                                                 Unit test: you
                                                                        write it
      @Test
      void cellChangeShouldPropagate() {
        // Arrange
        Spreadsheet sheet = new Spreadsheet();
        sheet.put("A1", "5");
        sheet.put("A2", "=A1");

          // Act                                         This is made
                                                        available from
          sheet.put("A1", "10");
                                                        the framework

          // Assert
          assertEquals("10", sheet.get("A2"));
      }

This example tests behavior, not implementation. It demonstrates the typical
structure: create object, perform actions, assert expected result.
                                           17 - JUnit
JUnit Concepts
• Test Method – a no-argument method of a java class
  (containing one or more test methods) annotated with @Test
     @Test
     public void someTestMethod() { … }

   • Test method contains business logic and assertions – checking if
     actual results equals expected results
   • In JUnit 5, test classes and test methods do not have to be public, but
     they must not be private.
• Test Case – Java class containing test methods
   • Fixture - the initial state of a Test Case
• Test Suite – collection of several Test Cases
                                      17 - JUnit
Topics

Unit testing
Junit overview

Junit test cases
Junit test methods
Junit test suites
Junit from IDE




                     17 - JUnit
Fixture: setUp/tearDown
• Sometimes tests need to run against the background of a
  known set of objects (fixture)
  • Annotate a method with @BeforeAll and initialize the variables in
    that method
  • Annotate a method with @AfterAll to release any permanent
    resources you allocated during set up
  • @BeforeAll / @AfterAll methods are only executed once for a
    given test class.
  • @BeforeAll / @AfterAll methods must have a void return type,
    must not be private, and must be static by default
        @BeforeAll static void setUp() {...}
        // Runs once before all tests

        @AfterAll static void tearDown() {...}
        // Runs once after all tests
                                  17 - JUnit
Test Lifecycle

Test-suite-wide initialization - class-scoped setUp() and tearDown()
methods
   • any method annotated @BeforeAll will run exactly once before the test
     methods in that class run
   • any method annotated with @AfterAll will run exactly once after all the
     tests in the class have been run




                                     17 - JUnit
Test Lifecycle
    @BeforeEach void setUp() {...}
          gets called once before each test method is
    executed
    @AfterEach void tearDown() {...}
           gets called once after each test method has been
           executed – regardless of whether the test passed
           or failed (or threw some other exception)
    @BeforeEach / @AfterEach methods must have a void
    return type, must not be private, and must not be static
      @BeforeEach void setUp() {...}
      // Runs before each test

      @AfterEach void tearDown() {...}
       // Runs after each test
                               17 - JUnit
SAMPLE TEST PROGRAM
           fixture



     fixture


    test


    test


     fixture



                     fixture   17 - JUnit
Topics

Unit testing

                     17 - JUnit
Assertions
• Assertions are used to check that actual test results are the
  same as expected results
• A set of assert methods is specified in
 https://junit.org/junit5/docs/current/api/org.junit.jupi
 ter.api/org/junit/jupiter/api/Assertions.html (see JUnit
  JavaDocs)
• The most often used assertions - assertEquals(), assertNull(),
  assertSame(), assertTrue(), assertNotNull, assertFalse,
  assertThrows, assertAll, assertIterableEquals - are enough for
  most situations
• fail() is used, if control should not reach that line in the test - this
  makes the test method to fail
• Prefer meaningful assertion messages when they help diagnose
  failures.
   • assertEquals(12, customers.size(), "Unexpected number of customers");
                                    17 - JUnit
Example
 import org.junit.jupiter.api.Assertions.*;

 import org.junit.jupiter.api.Test;
 import java.util.List;

 class CustomerServiceTest {
 {
   @Test
   void shouldReturnActiveCustomer() {
      List<Customer> customers = customerService.getAllCustomers();
      assertEquals(12, customers.size());

         Customer customer = customerService.getById("123");
         assertNotNull("Customer not found",       customer);

         boolean isActive =    customerService.isActive(customer);
         assertTrue("Customer is not active",      isActive);
     }
 }

                                   17 - JUnit
Testing Expected Exceptions
     • Write the code that throws the exception and
       simply use an annotation to declare that the
       exception is expected:
   @Test
   void
      shouldThrowArithmeticExceptionWhenDividingByZero()
      {
       assertThrows(ArithmeticException.class, () -> {
           int n = 2 / 0;
       });
   }

     • Test fails if exception will not be thrown
                             17 - JUnit
 Ignored Tests
    • Sometimes it’s useful to mark test to be ignored by test
      runner
       • test that takes an excessively long time to run
       • test that access remote network servers
       • test is failing for reasons beyond your control
    • Such tests can be annotated as @Disabled
    • Disabled tests should always include a reason and
      should not remain disabled indefinitely.
@Test
@Disabled("Temporarily disabled because remote service is unavailable")
void shouldCallRemoteService() {
    // test code
}
                                    17 - JUnit
Timed Tests
• Tests can be annotated with a timeout parameter
• If the test takes longer than the specified number of
  milliseconds to run, the test fails
• In JUnit 5, timeouts are handled using @Timeout
  @Test
  @Timeout(value = 500, unit = TimeUnit.MILLISECONDS)
  void retrieveAllElementsInDocument() {
      doc.query("//*");
  }

                @Test
                @Timeout(1)
                void shouldCompleteWithinOneSecond() {
                    // test code

Key points
 • Unit tests validate small parts of the code in isolation.
 • JUnit automates test execution for Java applications.
 • Test fixtures prepare and clean up test state.
 • Assertions verify expected behavior.
 • Tests should be fast, repeatable, independent, and readable.
 • Coverage helps identify untested code but does not guarantee test quality.

17/05/2026                            Lecture 16 - Software testing   2
Program testing
 • Testing is intended to show that a program does what it is
   intended to do and to discover program defects before it is put
   into use.
 • When you test software, you execute a program using artificial
   data.
 • You check the results of the test run for errors, anomalies or
   information about the program’s non-functional attributes.
 • Can reveal the presence of errors NOT their absence.
       • If tests fail → there is a defect.
       • If tests pass → you’ve only shown “no defect found under tested
         conditions,” not “defect-free.”
 • Testing is part of a more general verification and validation
   process, which also includes static validation techniques.
17/05/2026                       Lecture 16 - Software testing             3
Program testing: definitions
Error, Fault, Failure
• Error: a human mistake during requirements, design, or coding.
• Fault / Defect / Bug: the incorrect part of the software caused by
  an error.
• Failure: externally visible incorrect behavior during execution.

Example:
• Developer misunderstands a discount rule → error
• Wrong formula is coded → fault/defect
• Customer receives wrong final price → failure
17/05/2026                 Lecture 16 - Software testing           4
Program testing goals                                                   validation
                                                                         testing

• To demonstrate to the developer and the customer that the
  software meets its requirements.
     • For custom software, this means that there should be at least one test
       for every requirement in the requirements document. For generic
       software products, it means that there should be tests for all of the
       system features, plus combinations of these features, that will be
       incorporated in the product release. Does the normal scenario work?
• To discover situations in which the behavior of the software is
  incorrect, undesirable or does not conform to its specification.
     • Defect testing is concerned with rooting out undesirable system
       behavior such as system crashes, unwanted interactions with other
       systems, incorrect computations and data corruption. Can I make the
       system fail?                                                      defect
                                                                           testing
17/05/2026                      Lecture 16 - Software testing                   5
  Example: Flight booking system
Requirement:
“A passenger shall be able to pay for a selected flight using a valid credit card.”

Validation test:
• Select a valid flight
• Select a valid seat
• Pay with a valid card
• Expected result: booking is confirmed and ticket is issued

Defect test:
• Try payment with expired card
• Try payment twice using the same booking
• Try payment after seat becomes unavailable
• Try payment with amount = 0 or negative amount
• Expected result: system rejects invalid cases safely
17/05/2026                         Lecture 16 - Software testing                      6
Validation and defect testing
• The first goal leads to validation testing
     • You expect the system to perform correctly using a
       given set of test cases that reflect the system’s
       expected use. Validation tests answer: “Does it do
       what we promised?”
• The second goal leads to defect testing
     • The test cases are designed to expose defects. The
       test cases in defect testing can be deliberately
       obscure and need not reflect how the system is
       normally used. Defect tests answer: “How can we
       break it?”
17/05/2026               Lecture 16 - Software testing      7
 Testing process goals: success criterion
  Success means different things depending on the testing
                        objective
• Validation testing
      • To demonstrate to the developer and the customer that the
        software meets its requirements
      • A successful test shows that the system operates as intended.
• Defect testing
      • To discover faults or defects in the software where its behaviour
        is incorrect or not in conformance with its specification
      • A successful test is a test that makes the system perform
        incorrectly and so exposes a defect in the system.

17/05/2026                     Lecture 16 - Software testing                8
     V-model testing
     The Verification and Validation model links:
     development phases (left side of the V) → requirements, design, implementation
     with
     testing levels (right side of the V)→ unit/component tests, integration tests, system tests,
     acceptance tests.

In waterfall it
happens
according to v-

Testing isn’t “after

back to

     17/05/2026                            Lecture 16 - Software testing                            9
    Agile testing

• In agile model, testing
  happens multiple times
  (iteratively and/or
  incrementally).
• Each sprint/iteration
  includes development
  + testing. You validate                                   Acceptance
  continuously, not only                                    testing
  at the end.

    17/05/2026              Lecture 16 - Software testing     10
Regression Testing
Regression testing is essential in iterative development because
every sprint can introduce unintended side effects.
• Re-running previously passed tests after a change.
• Goal: ensure new changes did not break existing functionality.
• Especially important in agile and continuous integration.

For example, after adding PayPal payment:
• Re-run credit card payment tests.
• Re-run booking confirmation tests.
• Re-run ticket generation tests.
17/05/2026                Lecture 16 - Software testing            11
Acceptance Criteria
Acceptance criteria define when a user story is considered complete.

User story:
“As a passenger, I want to pay for my ticket so that I can confirm my
booking.”

Acceptance criteria:
• Payment can be completed with valid card.
• Booking status changes to PAID.
• Ticket is generated.
• Confirmation email is sent.
• Invalid payment is rejected with clear message.
17/05/2026                   Lecture 16 - Software testing              12
   An input-output model of program testing

Testing can be seen as:
• provide test inputs
• execute system
• observe outputs/behavior
• compare against expected
  results (oracle)

Testing is a controlled
experiment: you manipulate
inputs to reveal defects.
   17/05/2026                Lecture 16 - Software testing   13
Test case structure
Anatomy of a Test Case     Test ID: TC-PAY-001
• Test ID
                           Requirement:    Passenger pays for ticket
• Requirement / feature    Precondition: Booking exists with
tested                     selected flight and seat
• Preconditions            Input: Valid card, amount = €120
• Test input data          Steps:
• Execution steps
                             1. Open booking
• Expected result
• Actual result              2. Submit payment

17/05/2026                Lecture 16 - Software testing         14
Equivalence partitioning and boundary testing

 Selecting Test Inputs

 Equivalence partitioning:
 • Divide inputs into classes expected to behave similarly.
 • Test one representative value from each class.

 Boundary value analysis:
 • Defects often occur at boundaries.
 • Test values just below, at, and just above limits.
 17/05/2026              Lecture 16 - Software testing        15
Example
Requirement:
“Passenger age must be between 0 and 120.”

Equivalence partitions:
• Valid: 0–120
• Invalid: < 0
• Invalid: > 120

Boundary values:
• -1, 0, 1
• 119, 120, 121
17/05/2026                Lecture 16 - Software testing   16
V & V confidence
• Aim of V & V is to establish confidence that the system is ‘fit for
  purpose’.
• Depends on system’s purpose, user expectations and marketing
  environment
     • Software purpose
             • The level of confidence depends on how critical the software is to an organisation.
     • User expectations
             • Users may have low expectations of certain kinds of software.
     • Marketing environment
             • Getting a product to market early may be more important than finding defects in the
               program.
Testing strategy differs across products:
     • medical device vs mobile app
     • aircraft software vs game

17/05/2026                                 Lecture 16 - Software testing                             17
Inspections and testing

• Software inspections Concerned with analysis of
  the static system representation to discover
  problems (static verification)
     • May be supported by tool-based document and code
       analysis.



• Software testing Concerned with exercising and
  observing product behaviour (dynamic verification)
     • The system is executed with test data and its operational
       behaviour is observed.

17/05/2026                    Lecture 16 - Software testing        18
 Inspections and testing examples
Inspection examples:
• Review requirements for ambiguity.
• Review UML diagrams for missing associations.
• Review code for duplicated logic.
• Review test cases for missing edge cases.

Testing examples:
• Execute payment with valid card.
• Simulate network failure during payment.
• Measure response time under load.
17/05/2026                Lecture 16 - Software testing   19
Inspections and testing




17/05/2026      Lecture 16 - Software testing   20
Software inspections

• These involve people examining the source representation with
  the aim of discovering anomalies and defects.
• Inspections do not require execution of a system so may be
  applied before implementation.
• They may be applied to any representation/artifacts of the system
  (requirements, design, configuration data, test data, etc.).
• They have been shown to be an effective technique for
  discovering program errors.
• Inspections are an early defect detection technique.

17/05/2026                 Lecture 16 - Software testing         21
Advantages of inspections

• During testing, errors can mask (hide) other errors. Because
  inspection is a static process, you don’t have to be concerned
  with interactions between errors.
• Incomplete versions of a system can be inspected without
  additional costs. If a program is incomplete, then you need to
  develop specialized test harnesses to test the parts that are
  available.
• As well as searching for program defects, an inspection can
  also consider broader quality attributes of a program, such as
  compliance with standards, portability and maintainability.
17/05/2026                 Lecture 16 - Software testing           22
Inspections and testing
• Inspections and testing are complementary and not opposing
  verification techniques.
• Both should be used during the V & V process.
• Inspections can check conformance with a specification but not
  conformance with the customer’s real requirements.
• Inspections cannot check non-functional characteristics such as
  performance, usability, etc.
• Use inspections to catch defects in artifacts.
• Use testing to validate actual behavior and qualities.
• Neither replaces the other.
17/05/2026                Lecture 16 - Software testing         23
A model of the software testing process




17/05/2026      Lecture 16 - Software testing   24
Stages of testing
• Development testing, where the system is tested during
  development to discover bugs and defects.
• Release testing, where a separate testing team test a complete
  version of the system before it is released to users.
• User testing, where users or potential users of a system test the
  system in their own environment.
These stages differ primarily by:
• who performs them,
• when they happen, and
• what the objective is (developer feedback vs release confidence
  vs acceptance).
17/05/2026                 Lecture 16 - Software testing          25
V-model linking to testing stages
Testing exists at multiple levels and times:
• early (development)
• before release (release testing)
• in the real environment (user testing)


                                                                          User testing

                                                                       Release testing




                                                                          Dev testing


17/05/2026                             Lecture 16 - Software testing            26
White-box vs black-box testing
Tests can be derived from requirements and expected behavior,
or internal implementation structure.

Black-box testing
• Tests are designed from requirements/specification.
• Internal code structure is ignored.
• Example: test that payment rejects an expired card.

White-box testing
• Tests are designed using knowledge of the code.
• Focus on paths, branches, conditions, loops.
• Example: test all branches in calculateDiscount().
17/05/2026                 Lecture 16 - Software testing        27
Non-functional testing
• Performance testing  Response time, throughput,
scalability

• Security testing  Authentication, authorization, input
validation

• Usability testing  Ease of use, user satisfaction

• Reliability testing  Stability over time, failure recovery

• Compatibility testing  Browsers, devices, operating
systems
17/05/2026                Lecture 16 - Software testing         28
Test levels
• Unit testing : tests individual classes/functions.

• Integration testing: tests interactions between
components/services.

• System testing: tests the complete system against
requirements.

• Acceptance testing: tests whether the system is
acceptable to users/customers.
17/05/2026               Lecture 16 - Software testing   29
Takeaways
• Testing executes software with test data.
• Testing can reveal defects, not prove their absence.
• Validation testing checks that requirements are met.
• Defect testing tries to expose failures.
• Inspections are static; testing is dynamic.
• Both inspections and testing are needed.
• Testing happens at multiple levels: unit, integration, system,
acceptance.
• Agile requires continuous and regression testing.
• Good test design uses equivalence classes and boundary
values.
17/05/2026                 Lecture 16 - Software testing           30
Topics

  Introduction
  Develepment testing
                  unit testing
                  component testing
                  system testing
                  test-driven development
   Release testing
   User testing

17/05/2026                            Lecture 16 - Software testing   31
Development testing
• Development testing includes all testing activities that are
  carried out by the team developing the system.
     • Unit testing, where individual program units or object classes are
       tested. Unit testing should focus on testing the functionality of objects
       or methods. Unit testing checks whether a small piece of logic works
       correctly in isolation.
     • Component testing, where several individual units are integrated to
       create composite components. Component testing checks whether the
       units collaborate correctly. Component testing should focus on testing
       components interfaces.
     • System testing, where some or all of the components in a system are
       integrated and the system is tested as a whole, checking its behavior.
       System testing should focus on testing component interactions.

17/05/2026                       Lecture 16 - Software testing                 32
Development testing levels
1. Unit testing
  - Tests individual methods/classes.
  - Focus: functionality of objects or methods.
  - Example: test Seat.reserve().
2. Component testing
  - Tests integrated groups of units/classes.
  - Focus: component interfaces.
  - Example: test BookingService using SeatService and BookingRepository.
3. System testing
  - Tests the integrated system as a whole.
  - Focus: interactions between components.
  - Example: test full book-and-pay flight flow.
Notes:
- Unit tests are usually fast and numerous.
- Component tests may use mocks/stubs/fakes.
- System tests are fewer, slower, and closer to real user scenarios..
17/05/2026                         Lecture 16 - Software testing            33
Topics

  Introduction
  Develepment testing
                  unit testing
                  component testing
                  system testing
                  test-driven development
   Release testing
   User testing

17/05/2026                            Lecture 16 - Software testing   34
 Unit testing
• Unit testing is the process of testing individual components in
  isolation.
• It is mostly a defect testing process.
• Units may be:
     • Individual functions or methods within an object
     • Object classes with several attributes and methods
     A unit is the smallest piece of software that can be tested meaningfully.
“In isolation” means that when testing one unit, you try to reduce
interference from other parts of the system. For example, if you
test a PaymentService, you normally do not want to call a real
bank API. Instead, you use a fake or mock payment gateway.

17/05/2026                       Lecture 16 - Software testing                   35
         Example: flight booking domain
class Seat {
   private boolean reserved;

    public void reserve() {
      if (reserved) {
          throw new IllegalStateException("Seat already reserved");
      }
      reserved = true;
    }                                             @Test
                                                  void reserveShouldMarkSeatAsReserved() {
    public boolean isReserved() {                   Seat seat = new Seat();
        return reserved;
                                                          seat.reserve();
    }
}
                                                          assertTrue(seat.isReserved());
                                                     }
         17/05/2026                    Lecture 16 - Software testing                       36
Object class testing
• Complete test coverage of a class involves
     • Testing all operations associated with an object
     • Setting and interrogating all object attributes
     • Exercising the object in all possible states.
• Inheritance makes it more difficult to design object class tests as the
  information to be tested is not localised.
For object-oriented systems, testing a class means more than testing
one method. You must test:
All public operations. Example: reserve(), cancelReservation(),
isAvailable().
Attributes indirectly. Usually, attributes are private, so you test them
through public methods.
States. Objects often behave differently depending on internal state.
17/05/2026                         Lecture 16 - Software testing        37
Example: Booking states
A booking might have these states:
CREATED → FLIGHT_SELECTED → SEAT_SELECTED →
PAID → TICKET_ISSUED
Testing only one method is not enough. You should test whether:
• a booking can select a seat only after selecting a flight,
• a ticket can be issued only after payment,
• payment cannot be repeated after the booking is already paid.
If Passport extends IdDocument, some behavior is inherited from
IdDocument, while some behavior is implemented in Passport. To
fully test Passport, you must consider both: inherited methods,
overridden methods, subclass-specific behavior. Inheritance
complicates testing
17/05/2026               Lecture 16 - Software testing        38
       The weather station object interface
Unit testing an object class starts from its public interface: the methods
that other classes can call. When testing an object, you normally test
through its interface, not by directly manipulating internal private fields.
A good object-class testing strategy would ask:
• What are all the public methods?
• What state changes can happen?
• What are valid and invalid sequences of method calls?
• What happens if sensors return abnormal values?

Normal tests:
- Start weather station.
- Read temperature.
- Read wind speed.
- Generate weather report.
Abnormal tests:
- Read sensor before startup.
- Sensor returns missing value.
- Sensor returns extreme value.
- Calibration fails.
       17/05/2026                                  Lecture 16 - Software testing   39
Automated testing
• Whenever possible, unit testing should be automated so that
  tests are run and checked without manual intervention.
• In automated unit testing, you make use of a test automation
  framework (such as JUnit) to write and run your program tests.
• Unit testing frameworks provide generic test classes that you
  extend to create specific test cases. They can then run all of the
  tests that you have implemented and report, often through
  some GUI, on the success of otherwise of the tests.
Automation is essential because unit tests are executed many times: after
writing new code, after fixing bugs, after refactoring, before committing code,
in continuous integration pipelines. Manual unit testing is slow and
unreliable. Automated testing gives fast feedback.
17/05/2026                      Lecture 16 - Software testing                40
Test doubles
• Stub: returns predefined answers.

• Mock: verifies interactions.

• Fake: simplified working implementation.

• Dummy: passed but not used.

Example: use FakePaymentGateway instead of a real
bank API.
17/05/2026              Lecture 16 - Software testing   41
Automated test components
An automated test usually has three parts:
• A setup part, where you initialize the system with the test case, namely the inputs and
  expected outputs.
• A call part, where you call the object or method to be tested.
• An assertion part where you compare the result of the call with the expected result. If the
  assertion evaluates to true, the test has been successful if false, then it has failed.
This is often called the Arrange–Act–Assert structure.
@Test
void passportWithValidNumberShouldBeValid() {
  // Arrange / setup: prepare objects and inputs
  Passport passport = new Passport("P123456");
  // Act / call: call the method under test
  boolean result = passport.validate();
  // Assert: check the result
  assertTrue(result);
}
17/05/2026                            Lecture 16 - Software testing                             42
Example
@Test
void reserveAvailableSeatShouldSucceed() {
  // setup
  Seat seat = new Seat("12A");

    // call
    seat.reserve();

    // assertion
    assertTrue(seat.isReserved());
}

17/05/2026                   Lecture 16 - Software testing   43
Choosing unit test cases
• The test cases should show that, when used as expected, the
  component that you are testing does what it is supposed to do.
• If there are defects in the component, these should be revealed
  by test cases.
• This leads to 2 types of unit test case:
   • The first of these should reflect normal operation of a program and
     should show that the component works as expected.
   • The other kind of test case should be based on testing experience of
     where common problems arise. It should use abnormal inputs to check
     that these are properly processed and do not crash the component.
1. Valid passenger selects available seat. Valid passport is accepted. Valid
   payment amount is processed.
2. Passenger selects already reserved seat. Passport number is empty.
   Payment amount is negative. Booking ID does not exist.
17/05/2026                     Lecture 16 - Software testing               44
Testing strategies
Since we cannot test every possible input, how do we choose
useful inputs?
• Partition testing, where you identify groups of inputs that have
  common characteristics and should be processed in the same
  way.
     • You should choose tests from within each of these groups. Example:
       age input (invalid: age < 0; valid: 0 <= age <= 120; invalid: age > 120)
• Guideline-based testing, where you use testing guidelines to
  choose test cases.
     • These guidelines reflect previous experience of the kinds of errors that
       programmers often make when developing components. Example:
       known bug-prone cases (empty sequences, single-element lists,
       repeated inputs, very large values, invalid values, boundary values)
17/05/2026                       Lecture 16 - Software testing                    45
Partition testing
• Input data and output results often fall into different classes
  where all members of a class are related.
• Each of these classes is an equivalence partition or domain
  where the program behaves in an equivalent way for each class
  member.
• Test cases should be chosen from each partition.
If several inputs are expected to be handled in the same way, you
test a single representative input value from each partition.
Example: seat number validation. Suppose valid seat numbers
are: 1A to 30F. Partitions might be: Valid seat numbers (1A, 12C,
30F). Invalid seat numbers (0A, 31A, 12Z, empty string). You test
(at least) one input from each partition.
17/05/2026                Lecture 16 - Software testing        46
Equivalence partitioning




17/05/2026      Lecture 16 - Software testing   47
   Equivalence partitions
Boundary Value Analysis

Defects often occur at
boundaries between partitions.

Example:
Valid age: 0 - 120

Test:
-1, 0, 1
119, 120, 121


   17/05/2026                    Lecture 16 - Software testing   48
Testing guidelines for sequences
• Test software with sequences which have only a single value.
• Use sequences of different sizes in different tests.
• Derive tests so that the first, middle and last elements of the
  sequence are accessed.
• Test with sequences of zero length.

A sequence may be a list, an array, a queue, a string, a collection
of objects. Sequences are error-prone because of indexing and
boundary conditions (0 elements, 1 element, several elements,
access first element, access middle element, access last
element).
17/05/2026                 Lecture 16 - Software testing            49
General testing guidelines
• Choose inputs that force the system to generate all error
  messages
• Design inputs that cause input buffers to overflow
• Repeat the same input or series of inputs numerous times
• Force invalid outputs to be generated
• Force computation results to be too large or too small.




17/05/2026                Lecture 16 - Software testing       50
Unit tests qualities
Good unit tests should be:
• Fast
• Automated
• Repeatable
• Independent
• Easy to understand

FIRST acronym: Fast, Independent, Repeatable, Self-validating,
Timely
17/05/2026                   Lecture 16 - Software testing       51
Takeaways
• Unit testing checks individual functions, methods, or classes in
  isolation.
• Object class testing should cover operations, attributes, and
  states.
• Automated tests normally contain setup, call, and assertion.
• Unit tests should include both normal and abnormal cases.
• Partition testing selects representatives from input/output
  classes.
• Boundary values are especially important.
• Guideline-based testing uses known bug-prone cases.
• Sequence tests should include empty, single, small, large, first,
  middle, and last cases.
17/05/2026                 Lecture 16 - Software testing              52
Topics

  Introduction
  Develepment testing
                  unit testing
                  component testing
                  system testing
                  test-driven development
   Release testing
   User testing

17/05/2026                            Lecture 16 - Software testing   53
Component testing
• Software components are often composite components that are
  made up of several interacting objects.
     • For example, in the weather station system, the reconfiguration
       component includes objects that deal with each aspect of the
       reconfiguration.
• You access the functionality of these objects through the
  defined component interface.
• Testing composite components should therefore focus on
  showing that the component interface behaves according to its
  specification.
     • You can assume that unit tests on the individual objects within the
       component have been completed.
17/05/2026                       Lecture 16 - Software testing               54
Component interface as contract
In component testing, the focus becomes a group of collaborating
classes that together provide a useful service. For example, in a
flight booking system, a BookingComponent might internally
contain BookingService, SeatService, TicketService,
BookingRepository.
From outside, other parts of the system do not need to know
those internal objects. Other parts of the system use only the
public component interface.
Does the component, as accessed through its public interface,
behave correctly?
Component testing verifies the contract of a component. It checks
whether the component offers the correct behavior through its
interface, assuming that its internal units have already been unit-
tested
17/05/2026                 Lecture 16 - Software testing         55
Interface as a Contract
An interface should specify:
• Method/message name
• Parameter types and valid ranges
• Return values
• Exceptions/errors
• Preconditions
• Postconditions
• Side effects
• Timing expectations
17/05/2026               Lecture 16 - Software testing   56
Interface as a Contract: example
reserveSeat(bookingId, seatId)
Precondition:
- booking exists
- seat exists
- seat is available
Postcondition:
- seat is reserved for booking
- booking has selected seat
Errors:
- BookingNotFound
- SeatUnavailable
- InvalidSeat
17/05/2026                 Lecture 16 - Software testing   57
       Interface testing
Even if component A and component B both
work correctly alone, the system can fail when
A calls B incorrectly.
Example:
call: bookingService.reserveSeat("12A", "B123");
definition: reserveSeat(bookingId, seatId);
caller: reserveSeat(seatId, bookingId)
both components may be “correct” internally, but the interface
interaction is wrong.
Both arguments are strings, so the compiler may not detect
the mistake. The error appears at runtime.

Component integration creates new failure
points. Interface testing focuses on those
points.
      17/05/2026                                Lecture 16 - Software testing   61
Interface errors
• Interface misuse
     • A calling component calls another component and makes an error in its use of
       component interface e.g. parameters in the wrong order. Use stronger types to reduce
       this risk: reserveSeat(BookingId bookingId, SeatId seatId)
• Interface misunderstanding
     • A calling component embeds assumptions about the behaviour of the called
       component which are incorrect. Example: a payment component returns
       PaymentResult.PENDING and the caller assumes that payment succeeded because
       no exception is thrown. To reduce this risk write clear interface contracts, document
       preconditions and postconditions, test ambiguous cases, use explicit return types
       rather than vague booleans.
• Timing errors
     • The called and the calling component operate at different speeds and out-of-date
       information is accessed. Example: a seat availability component says: Seat 12A is
       available. But before the booking component reserves it, another user reserves the
       same seat. The booking component is working with outdated information. Common in
       concurrent systems, distributed systems, message passing systems, real-time
       systems, systems using caches. To test simulate delays, run concurrent requests, test
       retries, test stale data, test race conditions.
17/05/2026                            Lecture 16 - Software testing                        62
Interface testing guidelines
• Design tests so that parameters to a called procedure are at the
  extreme ends of their ranges.
• Always test pointer parameters with null pointers.
• Design tests which cause the component to fail (deliberately
  test negative cases to ensure failures are handled gracefully,
  not through crashes or data corruption).
• Use stress testing in message passing systems (this can reveal
  queue overflows, lost messages, duplicate processing, and
  timing defects).
• In shared memory systems, vary the order in which components
  are activated (if components share memory or shared state,
  bugs may depend on execution order).

17/05/2026                Lecture 16 - Software testing         63
   Component testing

testing                                      Test:
• Stub: returns predefined                   • missing parameters
responses.                                   • null parameters

implementation.                              • delayed responses

subsystem behavior.                          • retry behavior
                                             • partial failure
   17/05/2026                    Lecture 16 - Software testing                64
Takeaways
• Component testing checks groups of collaborating objects.
• The main focus is the component interface, not internal unit
behavior.
• Interface testing detects misuse, misunderstanding, and timing
errors.
• Interfaces may be parameter-based, shared-memory,
procedural, or message-based.
• Good interface tests include boundary values, nulls, failures,
stress, and concurrency cases.
• Test doubles help isolate components from external systems.

  Develepment testing

17/05/2026                            Lecture 16 - Software testing   66
System testing
At earlier levels:
• Unit testing checks isolated methods/classes.
• Component testing checks a group of related objects or a
  component interface.
• System testing checks the integrated system as a whole or as a
  large integrated subsystem.
The key idea is that a system may fail even if all individual
components pass their own tests.


17/05/2026                Lecture 16 - Software testing        67
System testing
For example, in a flight booking system:
• FlightSearchService may work correctly alone.
• SeatService may work correctly alone.
• PaymentService may work correctly alone.
• TicketService may work correctly alone.
But the system can still fail if:
• seat reservation is not completed before payment,
• payment confirmation does not trigger ticket generation,
• passenger document validation is skipped,
• the wrong booking ID is passed between services,
• a component sends data too late or in the wrong format.

17/05/2026                  Lecture 16 - Software testing    68
System testing
• System testing during development involves integrating
  components to create a version of the system and then testing
  the integrated system.
• The focus in system testing is testing the interactions between
  components.
• System testing checks that components are compatible, interact
  correctly and transfer the right data at the right time across their
  interfaces.
• System testing tests the emergent behaviour of a system.
• System testing focuses on whether components: call each other
  in the correct order, exchange the correct data, handle errors
  correctly, remain consistent after workflows complete, respect
  timing constraints.

17/05/2026                  Lecture 16 - Software testing            69
System and component testing
• During system testing, reusable components that have been
  separately developed and off-the-shelf systems may be
  integrated with newly developed components. The complete
  system is then tested.
• Components developed by different team members or sub-
  teams may be integrated at this stage. System testing is a
  collective rather than an individual process.
     • In some companies, system testing may involve a separate testing
       team with no involvement from designers and programmers.
System testing is not just technically bigger; it also involves more
                                                                 Not in the
people, more dependencies, and more coordination.                  agile
                                                                          model

17/05/2026                     Lecture 16 - Software testing                 70
Use-case testing
• The use-cases developed to identify system interactions can be
  used as a basis for system testing.
• Each use case usually involves several system components so
  testing the use case forces these interactions to occur.
• The sequence diagrams associated with the use case
  documents the components and interactions that are being
  tested.
A very effective way to design system tests is to start from use
cases or user stories. Because use cases describe user goals
that normally require multiple components to collaborate.
             Use case/user story �                    system test
17/05/2026                  Lecture 16 - Software testing           71
Use-case testing
Example: flight booking use case
As a passenger, I want to book and pay for a flight so that I can make my
trip.
A system test derived from this use case might execute:
Search flights.
Select flight.
Select seat.
Upload document.
Pay.
Issue ticket.
Send confirmation.
This forces interactions among: UI/API layer, Booking component, Flight
search component, Seat service, Document validation, Payment gateway,
Ticket service, Notification service.
This is exactly what system testing is meant to check.

Use cases and user stories both support system testing. Use
cases are usually more detailed; user stories need acceptance
criteria or additional documentation to become testable.
        17/05/2026                              Lecture 16 - Software testing                                        73
acceptance criteria for user stories
User story:
As a patient, I want my insulin level to be adjusted so that I stay
healthy.

Acceptance criteria:
- System reads current glucose level.
- System calculates insulin dose within safe limits.
- System logs the adjustment.
- System raises an alarm if sensor data is missing.
- System never delivers a dose above maximum safe threshold.
17/05/2026                    Lecture 16 - Software testing           74
use case vs user story
Traditional requirements engineering often uses detailed use cases.
Agile uses shorter user stories:
As a [role],
I want [goal],
so that [benefit].
User stories are intentionally lightweight but sometimes user stories
need to be detailed. That usually happens when: the domain is safety-
critical, the workflow is complex, there are many edge cases, external
systems are involved, acceptance criteria must be precise.
A user story alone is often not enough to create a complete system
test. You normally need acceptance criteria that can become system
tests.

Section                Description
                       States the use case name. Typically, the name expresses the objective or observable result of the use case, such as “Withdraw Cash” in the case of
Use case name
                       an automatic teller machine or «Book Flight» in the case of booking flight system.

                       Presents the basic flow and alternative flows. The flow of events describes the behavior of the system; it does not describe how the system works,
                       the details of the presentation, or the details of the user interface. If information is exchanged, the use case must be specific about what is passed
Flow of events         back and forth. For example, instead of describing an action as “the actor enters customer information”, indicate that “the actor enters the customer
                       name and address”. For example: 1. Passenger selects a flight. 2. System displays available seats. 3. Passenger selects a seat. 4. System reserves
                       the seat.
                       Describes the ideal, primary behavior of the system. For example: Passenger selects available flight, selects available seat, pays successfully,

                       receives ticket.

                       Describes exceptions or deviations from the basic flow, such as how the system behaves when the actor enters an incorrect user ID and the user
Alternative flows

Special requirements

                       issued. Confirmation is sent.


Extension points       A point in the use case flow of events at which another use case is referenced. For example: During Book Flight, extension point: Pay Ticket.

17/05/2026                      Lecture 16 - Software testing        83
Example system test: Book and pay flight
Preconditions:
- Passenger exists.
- Flight F123 has available seats.
- Payment gateway test environment is available.
Steps:
1. Search flight.
2. Select flight F123.
3. Select seat 12A.
4. Upload valid passport.
5. Pay with valid card.
Expected results:
- Booking status = PAID.
- Seat 12A is reserved.
- Ticket is issued.
- Confirmation is sent.


17/05/2026                              Lecture 16 - Software testing   84
Add normal and alternative flows
Use-case tests should not test only the happy path.
Use-case testing should cover:

Main success scenario:
- passenger books and pays successfully.

Alternative flows:
- selected seat becomes unavailable.
- document validation fails.
- payment is declined.
- payment provider times out.
- booking is cancelled before payment.
17/05/2026                  Lecture 16 - Software testing   85
Testing policies
• Exhaustive system testing is impossible so testing policies
  which define the required system test coverage may be
  developed.
• Examples of testing policies:
     • All system functions that are accessed through menus should be
       tested.
     • Combinations of functions (e.g. text formatting) that are accessed
       through the same menu must be tested.
     • Where user input is provided, all functions must be tested with both
       correct and incorrect input.


17/05/2026                       Lecture 16 - Software testing                86
Testing policies
• Which features must be tested?
• Which combinations must be tested?
• Which user roles must be tested?
• Which input types must be tested?
• Which platforms must be tested?
• Which risks require special attention?
1. Every user-visible function must have at least one system test.
2. Every payment method must be tested with valid and invalid inputs.
3. Every booking workflow must be tested for success and cancellation.
4. Seat selection must be tested for available, unavailable, and already-reserved seats.
5. Critical flows must be tested on every release.
6. Non-critical flows may be tested once per major release.
17/05/2026                           Lecture 16 - Software testing                         87
Takeaways
• System testing checks the integrated system.
• The main focus is interaction between components.
• System testing may reveal emergent behavior not visible in
unit/component tests.
• It often integrates new, reused, and external components.
• Use cases and user stories are good sources for system test cases.
• Sequence diagrams help identify the component interactions being
tested.
• User stories need acceptance criteria to become testable.
• System tests should include main flows, alternative flows, and failure
scenarios.

  Develepment testing
                  unit testing
                  component testing
                  system testing

   Release testing
   User testing

17/05/2026                            Lecture 16 - Software testing   89
Test-driven development
• Test-driven development (TDD) is an approach to program
  development in which you inter-leave testing and code
  development.
• Tests are written before code and ‘passing’ the tests is the
  critical driver of development.
• You develop code incrementally, along with a test for that
  increment. You don’t move on to the next increment until the
  code that you have developed passes its test.
• TDD was introduced as part of agile methods such as Extreme
  Programming. However, it can also be used in plan-driven
  development processes.

17/05/2026               Lecture 16 - Software testing           90
Test-driven development




• You write tests before writing code
• Development is driven by passing tests
• Work is done in small increments
• Each increment has a test to be passed before moving on (Unit
  testing is the main focus of TDD)
    “Code exists only to satisfy tests.”
17/05/2026                   Lecture 16 - Software testing        91
TDD process activities
• Start by identifying the increment of functionality that is required.
  This should normally be small and implementable in a few lines of
  code.
• Write a test for this functionality and implement this as an automated
  test.
• Run the test, along with all other tests that have been implemented.
  Initially, you have not implemented the functionality so the new test
  will fail.
• Implement the functionality and re-run the test.
• Once all tests run successfully, you move on to implementing the
  next chunk of functionality.
• Red–Green–Refactor cycle:          Red → failing test    Green → test
  passes       Refactor → improve code safely
17/05/2026                   Lecture 16 - Software testing             92
Benefits of test-driven development
• Code coverage
     • Every code segment that you write has at least one associated test so all
       code written has at least one test.
• Regression testing
     • A regression test suite is developed incrementally as a program is developed.
• Simplified debugging
     • When a test fails, it should be obvious where the problem lies. The newly
       written code needs likely to be checked and modified.
• System documentation
     • The tests themselves are a form of documentation that describe what the
       code should be doing.

17/05/2026                         Lecture 16 - Software testing                   93
A simple example
# Test first
def test_add():
   assert add(2, 3) == 5

# Then implement
def add(a, b):
  return a + b


17/05/2026                 Lecture 16 - Software testing   94
Regression testing
• Regression testing is testing the system to check that changes
  have not ‘broken’ previously working code.
• In a manual testing process, regression testing is expensive
  but, with automated testing, it is simple and straightforward. All
  tests are rerun every time a change is made to the program.
• Tests must run ‘successfully’ before the change is committed.




17/05/2026                  Lecture 16 - Software testing              95
Topics

  Introduction
  Develepment testing
                  unit testing
                  component testing
                  system testing
                  test-driven development
   Release testing
   User testing

17/05/2026                            Lecture 16 - Software testing   96
Release testing                                                                       Convince the
                                                                                     supplier, not the
                                                                                        customer!
        • Release testing is the process of testing a particular release of
          a system that is intended for use outside of the development
          team.
        • The primary goal of the release testing process is to convince
          the supplier of the system that it is good enough for use.
             • Release testing, therefore, has to show that the system delivers its
               specified functionality, performance and dependability, and that it does
               not fail during normal use.

        • Release testing is usually a black-box testing process where
          tests are only derived from the system specification.
             • Black-box = no knowledge of code
             • Based only on inputs/outputs and requirements

17/05/2026                              Lecture 16 - Software testing                         97
Release testing and system testing
• Release testing is a form of system testing.
• Important differences:
     • A separate team that has not been involved in the system
       development, should be responsible for release testing.
     • System testing by the development team should focus on discovering
       bugs in the system (defect testing). The objective of release testing is
       to check that the system meets its requirements and is good enough
       for external use (validation testing).
• System testing = “what’s broken?”
• Release testing = “is this good enough to ship?”

17/05/2026                       Lecture 16 - Software testing                    98
Requirements based testing
• Requirements-based testing involves examining each
  requirement and developing a test or tests for it.
• Mentcare system requirements:
     • If a patient is known to be allergic to any particular medication, then
       prescription of that medication shall result in a warning message being
       issued to the system user.
     • If a prescriber chooses to ignore an allergy warning, they shall provide
       a reason why this has been ignored.

• Every requirement is verified explicitly
     • Unexpected user behavior may be missed
     • Depending on quality of requirements

17/05/2026                       Lecture 16 - Software testing                99
Requirements tests
Test cases derived from requirements
• Set up a patient record with no known allergies. Prescribe medication for allergies
  that are known to exist. Check that a warning message is not issued by the
  system.
• Set up a patient record with a known allergy. Prescribe the medication to that the
  patient is allergic to and check that the warning is issued by the system.
• Set up a patient record in which allergies to two or more drugs are recorded.
  Prescribe both of these drugs separately and check that the correct warning for
  each drug is issued.
• Prescribe two drugs that the patient is allergic to. Check that two warnings are
  correctly issued.
• Prescribe a drug that issues a warning and overrule that warning. Check that the
  system requires the user to provide information explaining why the warning was
  overruled.
Turn requirements into real scenarios you can execute.
17/05/2026                        Lecture 16 - Software testing                   100
A usage scenario for the Mentcare system
             George is a nurse who specializes in mental healthcare. One of his responsibilities is to visit
             patients at home to check that their treatment is effective and that they are not suffering from
             medication side effects.
             On a day for home visits, George logs into the Mentcare system and uses it to print his
             schedule of home visits for that day, along with summary information about the patients to
             be visited. He requests that the records for these patients be downloaded to his laptop. He is
             prompted for his key phrase to encrypt the records on the laptop.
             One of the patients that he visits is Jim, who is being treated with medication for depression.
             Jim feels that the medication is helping him but believes that it has the side effect of keeping
             him awake at night. George looks up Jim’s record and is prompted for his key phrase to
             decrypt the record. He checks the drug prescribed and queries its side effects.
             Sleeplessness is a known side effect so he notes the problem in Jim’s record and suggests
             that he visits the clinic to have his medication changed. Jim agrees so George enters a
             prompt to call him when he gets back to the clinic to make an appointment with a physician.
             George ends the consultation and the system re-encrypts Jim’s record.
             After, finishing his consultations, George returns to the clinic and uploads the records of
             patients visited to the database. The system generates a call list for George of those
             patients who He has to contact for follow-up information and make clinic appointments.
17/05/2026                                        Lecture 16 - Software testing                                 101
Features tested by scenario
• Authentication by logging on to the system.
• Downloading and uploading of specified patient records to a
  laptop.
• Home visit scheduling.
• Encryption and decryption of patient records on a mobile
  device.
• Record retrieval and modification.
• Links with the drugs database that maintains side-effect
  information.
• The system for call prompting.
One scenario tests many integrated features

17/05/2026                Lecture 16 - Software testing         102
Performance testing
• Part of release testing may involve testing the emergent
  properties of a system, such as performance and reliability.
• Tests should reflect the profile of use of the system.
• Performance tests usually involve planning a series of tests
  where the load is steadily increased until the system
  performance becomes unacceptable.
• Stress testing is a form of performance testing where the
  system is deliberately overloaded to test its failure behaviour.


17/05/2026                  Lecture 16 - Software testing            103
Takeaways
Unit Testing → Integration Testing → System Testing → Release Testing


Release testing → readiness for delivery
Requirements testing → validates specs
Scenario testing → validates real usage
Performance testing → validates system quality




17/05/2026                   Lecture 16 - Software testing              104
Topics

  Introduction
  Develepment testing
                  unit testing
                  component testing
                  system testing
                  test-driven development
   Release testing
   User testing

17/05/2026                            Lecture 16 - Software testing   105
User testing

• User or customer testing is a stage in the testing process in
  which users or customers provide input and advice on system
  testing.
• User testing is essential, even when comprehensive system
  and release testing have been carried out.
     • The reason for this is that influences from the user’s working
       environment have a major effect on the reliability, performance,
       usability and robustness of a system. These cannot be replicated in a
       testing environment.
• Only users can validate usability, realism, and practical
  behavior.
17/05/2026                      Lecture 16 - Software testing                  106
                                                                    Convince the

Types of user testing
                                                                  customer – not for
                                                                        agile



• Alpha testing - early feedback
     • Users of the software work with the development team to test the
       software at the developer’s site.
• Beta testing - discover real-world issues
     • A release of the software is made available to users to allow them to
       experiment and to raise problems that they discover with the system
       developers.
• Acceptance testing
     • Customers test a system to decide whether or not it is ready to be
       accepted from the system developers and deployed in the customer
       environment. Primarily for custom systems.
17/05/2026                       Lecture 16 - Software testing                  107
The acceptance testing process




             Acceptance is a formal decision process, not just testing.

17/05/2026                            Lecture 16 - Software testing       108
Stages in the acceptance testing process
• Define acceptance criteria
• Plan acceptance testing
• Derive acceptance tests
• Run acceptance tests
• Negotiate test results
• Reject/accept system

Unit → Component → System → Release → User Testing →
Acceptance
17/05/2026                 Lecture 16 - Software testing   109
Agile methods and acceptance testing
• In agile methods, the user/customer is part of the development
  team and is responsible for making decisions on the
  acceptability of the system.
• Tests are defined by the user/customer and are integrated with
  other tests that are run automatically when changes are made.
• There is no separate acceptance testing process.
• Main problem here is whether or not the embedded user is
  ‘typical’ and can represent the interests of all system
  stakeholders.

17/05/2026                Lecture 16 - Software testing        110
Key points
 • Testing can only show the presence of errors in a program. It cannot
   demonstrate that there are no remaining faults.
 • Development testing is the responsibility of the software development
   team. A separate team may be responsible for testing a system before it is
   released to customers.
 • Development testing includes unit testing, in which you test individual
   objects and methods component testing in which you test related groups
   of objects and system testing, in which you test partial or complete
   systems.
Key points
 • When testing software, you should try to ‘break’ the software by using
   experience and guidelines to choose types of test case that have been
   effective in discovering defects in other systems.
 • Wherever possible, you should write automated tests. The tests are
   embedded in a program that can be run every time a change is made to a
   system.
 • Test-first development is an approach to development where tests are
   written before the code to be tested.
 • Scenario testing involves inventing a typical usage scenario and using this
   to derive test cases.
 • Acceptance testing is a user testing process where the aim is to decide if
   the software is good enough to be deployed and used in its operational
   environment.
